Opponents' Classification of Visibles Refuted
Presents opponents' classification and asserts its invalidity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponents' classification of visible objects and why the author declares it invalid.

Core Idea
Vision does not require spatial opposition or being in a state of opposition.

The author begins by presenting the classification of visible objects proposed by his opponents. They divide visibles into three categories: bodies, accidents, and images in mirrors. He then asserts that God sees all things without these conditions and declares the classification false. This lesson examines that initial claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. It states the opponents' classification and the author's declaration of falsehood.
Notice the three categories: opposite, in state of opposition, and having ruling of opposition.
2
Identify the three categories
List the three types of visible objects according to the opponents. Compare them to the translation.
3
Reflect on the author's claim
Why does the author say 'this speech is false'? What reasons might he have? This sets up the detailed refutation in the next lesson.
The author asserts that God sees in ways not limited to these conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يبطل قولهم : إن المرئي إما أن يكون مقابلا كالجسم أو حالا في المقابلة كالألوان والأكوان أو ما له حكم المقابلة كالمرئى في المرآة من صور الأشياء. فإن الله تعالى يرى المرئيات لا بهذه الوجوه على أن هذا الكلام باطل فإن المقابلة لو كانت شرطا لكان العرض لا يرى ولو كان الحلول في المقابلة شرطا لكان لا ترى الجواهر ولا ما له حكم المقابلة حكم المقابلة، ولو كان كونه في حال المقابلة شرطا لكان المقابل والحال في المقابل لا يريان، ورؤية كل شيء من هذه الأشياء مع انعدام ما اقترن بصاحبه دل أن شيئا من ذلك ليس بشرط، ولو شرط اجتماعها لكان لا يرى شيء البتة، لاستحالة اجتماعها، ولو جعل أحد هذه الأشياء شرطا لا محالة فلابد لهم من إقامة الدليل ولا دليل لهم سوى الوجود وهذا باطل على ما بينا.
And by this, their statement is invalidated: that the visible is either opposite like a body, or in a state of opposition like colors and accidents, or what has the ruling of opposition like the images of things seen in a mirror. For God sees visible things not in these ways. Moreover, this speech is false, for if opposition were a condition, then the accident would not be seen; and if being in a state of opposition were a condition, then substances and what has the ruling of opposition would not be seen; and if its being in a state of opposition were a condition, then the opposite and that which is in a state of opposition would not be seen; and the vision of every one of these things with the absence of what is associated with its counterpart indicates that none of that is a condition. And if their combination were made a condition, then nothing would be seen at all, due to the impossibility of their combination; and if one of these things were made a condition necessarily, then they must provide a proof, and they have no proof other than existence, and this is false as we have shown.
Source pages p. 643

Commentary
This passage refutes the opponents' classification of visible objects into three categories: those opposite (like bodies), those in a state of opposition (like accidents), and those having the ruling of opposition (like images in mirrors). The author argues that if any of these were a condition for vision, then many things we actually see would not be seen. For example, if opposition were a condition, accidents (like colors) would not be seen because they are not opposite in themselves. If being in a state of opposition were a condition, then substances (jawahir) would not be seen. If being in a state of opposition were a condition for the opposite, then both the opposite and the thing in opposition would not be seen. Since we do see these things, none of these are conditions. Moreover, if all conditions were required, nothing could be seen because they cannot coexist. The opponents have no proof other than mere existence, which is invalid.

Key Terms
المقابلة (opposition)
الجسم (body)
العرض (accident)
الجوهر (substance)
الحلول في المقابلة (being in a state of opposition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Visible objects must be spatially opposite or in a state of opposition to be seen.
Correction: The author argues that God sees all things without these conditions, and subsequent reasoning shows these conditions are not necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the opponents, which category do colors and accidents belong to?
A) Opposite
B) In a state of opposition
C) Having ruling of opposition
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The opponents classify visibles into three categories, but the author declares this false because God sees without such conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that God sees you at all times. But do you live as if His vision is limited by distance, time, or concealment? Where in your life do you act as if God does not see, because you think you can hide?
?
The opponents placed conditions on divine vision (opposition, etc.). What false conditions do you unconsciously place on God's knowledge of your actions—believing He doesn't see when you are alone or when no one is watching?
?
If you truly internalized that God's vision requires no physical conditions, what specific sin would you immediately stop committing? Write down one and commit to leaving it today.

Detailed Refutation of Conditions for Vision
Refutes each condition individually and the combination, and notes opponents have no proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses conditional reasoning to refute each proposed condition for vision and the impossibility of combining them.

Core Idea
If any condition were necessary, many things we see would be invisible.

After declaring the classification false, the author provides detailed reasoning. He tests each condition individually using a conditional argument: if X were a condition, then Y would not be seen. Since we do see Y, X cannot be a condition. He also considers the possibility of combining conditions and shows that would make nothing visible. This lesson unpacks these logical refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. It contains several conditional statements and a final challenge.
Notice the pattern: 'if ... were a condition, then ... would not be seen'.
2
Identify each conditional argument
List each condition tested: opposition, being in a state of opposition, and the third nuanced condition. What is the consequence for each?
3
Understand the pattern
The author shows that the predicted non-vision contradicts our actual experience. Therefore, the condition is invalid.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Consider the combination and lack of proof
The author also argues that combining conditions leads to impossibility, and opponents have no valid proof. Reflect on why these are powerful refutations.

Source Text

The author argues that if any of these were a condition for vision, then many things we actually see would not be seen. For example, if opposition were a condition, accidents (like colors) would not be seen because they are not opposite in themselves. If being in a state of opposition were a condition, then substances (jawahir) would not be seen. Misconception: The opponents might think that spatial conditions are necessary for any vision, including divine vision.
Correction: The author shows that these conditions cannot be necessary because that would lead to contradictions with observed vision.

A) Only bodies would be seen
B) Only accidents would be seen
C) Nothing would be seen

The author uses conditional arguments to show that each proposed condition leads to contradiction, and the opponents lack valid proof.

Refutation of Linguistic and Analogical Objections to Vision
The author refutes linguistic and analogical objections and concludes that direction is not intrinsic to vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes linguistic and analogical objections to the vision of God, and why direction and physical conditions are not intrinsic to vision.

Core Idea
Vision of God is not dependent on physical conditions like direction or opposition.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi addresses two final objections raised by those who deny the possibility of seeing God. The first objection is linguistic: the term 'vision' in Arabic implies certain physical conditions that cannot apply to God. The second is analogical: knowledge of the unseen must resemble knowledge of the witnessed. The author refutes both by showing that vision is defined by the act itself, not by accidental concomitants. Let's examine his arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the two objections being refuted.
The passage uses technical terms like 'القرائن الاتفاقية'.
2
Examine the first (linguistic) objection
The opponents claim that vision requires conditions like opposition, alignment, etc. Find how the author responds: he says these are only coincidental concomitants.
The author argues that vision is vision without them.
3
Examine the second (analogical) objection
The opponents draw an analogy with knowledge: the unseen must follow the witnessed. Find the author's counter: the same applies to vision, but the conditions are not necessary.
He points out that we see bodies in directions because they are in directions, not because direction is intrinsic to vision.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that God, being not finite and without direction, sees without direction. This establishes that vision of God is possible and different from physical vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يبطل قولهم : إن ما تدعونه أنه رؤية ليس برؤية إذ هي لا تعرف عند أهل اللغة فإن الله تعالى يرى ورؤيته ليست بمقابلة ولا محاذاة ولا اتصال شعاع ولا تقليب مقلة. وتبين أن هذه الأشياء ليست إلا من القرائن الاتفاقية والأوصاف الوجودية ، والرؤية رؤية بدونها. وكذا قولهم : إن العلم بالغائب لا يخرج عن الوجه الذي به يعلم الشاهد. قلنا : وكذا الرؤية فإنها كما كانت في حق الشاهد ضرورة كانت في حق الغائب كذا. وما ذكر من المسافة والمقابلة واتصال الشعاع قد أبطلنا أن يكون شيء منه من لوازم الرؤية بل كل شيء يرى كما هو وعلى ما هو، فلما كانت الأجسام في الجهات منا وبيننا وبينها قواء رأيناها كما هي وعلى ما هي عليه، لا لأن الجهة والمقابلة من لوازم الرؤية. والله تعالى ليس بمتناه ولا منا بجهة فيرى كما هو يرانا هو لا عن جهة ولا في جهة.
And by this, their statement is invalidated: that what you claim is vision is not vision, since it is not known among linguists. For God sees, and His vision is not by opposition, nor alignment, nor connection of rays, nor turning of the pupil. And it is shown that these things are only coincidental concomitants and existential attributes, and vision is vision without them. And likewise their statement: that knowledge of the unseen does not depart from the manner by which the witnessed is known. We say: likewise vision, for just as it was in the case of the witnessed necessarily, it is in the case of the unseen likewise. And what was mentioned of distance, opposition, and connection of rays, we have already nullified that any of it is among the necessary concomitants of vision; rather, everything is seen as it is and according to what it is. Since bodies are in directions relative to us and between us and them there is void, we see them as they are and as they are, not because direction and opposition are among the necessary concomitants of vision. And God is not finite nor in a direction relative to us, so He sees as He is, as He sees us, not by direction nor in direction.
Source pages p. 644

Commentary
This passage addresses two final objections. First, the opponents claim that the vision we affirm for God is not called 'vision' in language because it lacks conditions like opposition, alignment, etc. The author responds that vision is independent of these accidental features; they are merely coincidental. Second, the opponents draw an analogy with knowledge, arguing that knowledge of the unseen must follow the same pattern as knowledge of the witnessed. The author retorts that the same applies to vision: just as vision in the witnessed world occurs necessarily (under certain conditions), in the unseen it occurs without those conditions. The author concludes by reiterating that distance, opposition, and rays are not essential to vision. We see bodies in directions because they are in directions relative to us, but that does not mean direction is intrinsic to vision. God, being unlimited and without direction, sees without direction, just as He sees us without direction.

Key Terms
الرؤية (vision)
اللغة (language)
القرائن الاتفاقية (coincidental concomitants)
العلم بالغائب (knowledge of the unseen)
المشاهد (the witnessed)
المسافة (distance)
اتصال الشعاع (connection of rays)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that vision of God in the afterlife will be exactly like physical vision in this world, requiring conditions like direction and distance.
Correction: The passage clarifies that physical conditions such as opposition, alignment, and distance are only coincidental concomitants of vision in the created world. They are not essential to the act of vision itself. God's vision is without direction or any physical condition.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the claim that vision of God is not called 'vision' linguistically?
A) He argues that linguistic definitions are irrelevant to theological reality.
B) He says that vision is defined by the act itself, not by accidental conditions like opposition or direction.
C) He cites a dictionary definition that includes God's vision.
D) He agrees and uses a different term.

Key Takeaway
The vision of God is not dependent on physical conditions like direction, opposition, or rays; these are merely coincidental concomitants in the created world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you accept that God's vision of you is not limited by space or direction, and yet you often act as if He does not see you in your private moments? How can you cultivate a constant awareness of His vision without needing physical signs as evidence?
?

Are there aspects of your faith that you have made dependent on physical, worldly conditions (like prayer being 'effective' only in a quiet room)? What would it mean for you to worship God without relying on such conditions?

5 min
Vision of God Does Not Require Physical Conditions
Nasafi refutes that facing, distance, or direction are necessary for vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that conditions like facing, alignment, and ray connection are not necessary for seeing God, and how this defends the possibility of beatific vision.

Core Idea
God's vision is unlike physical vision; it needs no direction or distance.

In this passage, Nasafi tackles a common objection: that vision requires certain physical conditions such as facing, alignment, or a ray. He shows these are merely incidental to physical sight and cannot be applied to God. This clears the way for affirming that God can be seen without these limitations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Nasafi systematically lists and refutes each condition.
The passage uses a series of conditional arguments (if...then...).
2
Identify the conditions refuted
List the conditions that Nasafi mentions: confrontation, alignment, connection of a ray, rotation of eyeball. See how he argues against each.
He points out contradictions and lack of evidence.
3
Understand the refutation logic
Nasafi uses three arguments: (1) if confrontation were a condition, confronting parties wouldn't see each other; (2) seeing occurs even when some conditions are absent; (3) if all were required, nothing would be seen since they can't combine. Then he notes opponents have no proof.
The argument is based on absurd consequences.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Nasafi concludes that these conditions are merely coincidental (قرائن اتفاقية) and not essential. Thus vision of God does not require them.
This defends the theological doctrine that God can be seen in the afterlife without physical limitations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكم المقابلة، ولو كان كونه في حال المقابلة شرطا لكان المقابل والحال في المقابل لا يريان، ورؤية كل شيء من هذه الأشياء مع انعدام ما اقترن بصاحبه دل أن شيئا من ذلك ليس بشرط، ولو شرط اجتماعها لكان لا يرى شيء البتة، لاستحالة اجتماعها، ولو جعل أحد هذه الأشياء شرطا لا محالة فلابد لهم من إقامة الدليل ولا دليل لهم سوى الوجود وهذا باطل على ما بينا. وبهذا يبطل قولهم : إن ما تدعونه أنه رؤية ليس برؤية إذ هي لا تعرف عند أهل اللغة فإن الله تعالى يرى ورؤيته ليست بمقابلة ولا محاذاة ولا اتصال شعاع ولا تقليب مقلة. وتبين أن هذه الأشياء ليست إلا من القرائن الاتفاقية والأوصاف الوجودية () ، والرؤية رؤية بدونها.
The ruling on confrontation: if being in a state of confrontation were a condition, then the one confronting and the one in a state of confrontation would not see each other. And the seeing of every one of these things when what is associated with its companion is absent indicates that none of that is a condition. And if their combination were a condition, nothing at all would be seen, because their combination is impossible. And if one of these things is necessarily made a condition, they must produce evidence, and they have no evidence except existence, and that is false as we have shown. And by this their statement is refuted: 'What you claim to be vision is not vision, for it is not known among the people of language.' Indeed, God the Exalted is seen, and His seeing is not by confrontation, nor alignment, nor connection of a ray, nor rotation of an eyeball. And it is shown that these things are only coincidental concomitants and existential attributes, and vision is vision without them.
Source pages p. 644

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who claim that vision necessarily requires certain physical conditions such as confrontation (المقابلة), alignment (المحاذاة), connection of a ray (اتصال الشعاع), and rotation of the eyeball (تقليب المقلة). The argument proceeds by noting that if confrontation were a condition, then the confronting and the confronted would not see each other (implying a contradiction). Then it observes that seeing occurs even when these conditions are absent for some objects, showing they are not necessary. If all were required, nothing would be seen because they cannot combine. The opponents have no proof except sheer existence, which is invalid. Thus Nasafi concludes that these conditions are merely coincidental, not essential to vision. God is seen without them, and the term 'vision' in language does not require these physical contingencies.

Key Terms
الرؤية
المقابلة
المحاذاة
اتصال الشعاع
تقليب المقلة
القرائن الاتفاقية
الأوصاف الوجودية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vision necessarily requires physical direction and distance.
Correction: Physical conditions like facing and alignment are only incidental to physical sight; God's vision does not require them.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what indicates that confrontation is not a condition for vision?
A) The fact that confronting and confronted cannot see each other if it were a condition.
B) The fact that objects are seen even when the associated condition is absent.
C) The impossibility of combining all conditions.
D) The lack of evidence from the opponents.

Key Takeaway
God can be seen without physical conditions; the conditions of physical sight are merely incidental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you realize that your habit of imagining God in physical terms—like a direction or distance—is a subtle form of shirk? How has this lesson exposed that tendency in your own heart? What concrete step will you take today to purify your concept of God from such limitations?
?
When you think of 'seeing God' in the afterlife, do you subconsciously picture it as a physical sight? This passage demands you abandon that. Are you willing to accept that God's reality entirely surpasses your sensory expectations? What comfort or discomfort does that bring you?
?
The opponents had no evidence for their conditions except 'existence'—meaning they assumed physical laws are absolute. Where in your own life do you impose worldly limitations on God without proof? Name one such assumption and commit to replacing it with what this lesson teaches: that God is seen without conditions.

Analogy Between Knowledge and Vision
Nasafi analogizes the vision of the unseen God to knowledge of the unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses the analogy between knowledge and vision to defend the possibility of seeing Allah without spatial or physical conditions.

Core Idea
God's vision is not bound by the conditions of created vision.

In this passage, Nasafi addresses a criticism that knowledge of the unseen must follow the same pattern as knowledge of the seen. He counters by drawing an analogy with vision: just as knowledge of the unseen does not require sensory conditions, so too vision of God is not conditioned by distance, direction, or physical rays. This clarifies that God can be seen as He is, without spatial limitations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Nasafi uses a very compressed argumentative style.
2
Identify the objection and the analogy
Find the opponent's claim about knowledge and Nasafi's counter using vision. Understand how the analogy works: just as knowledge of the unseen doesn't replicate the conditions of witnessed knowledge, vision of God doesn't need the conditions of created vision.
The key phrase is 'قلنا : وكذا الرؤية' (we say: and similarly vision).
3
Analyze the refutation of vision conditions
Focus on the part where Nasafi dismisses distance, confrontation, and ray connection as necessary for vision. He explains that bodies in space are seen in a direction due to their nature, not because direction is essential to vision itself. God, being infinite and not in a direction, is seen without such conditions.
This refutes the misconception that divine vision requires physical proximity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا قولهم : إن العلم بالغائب لا يخرج عن الوجه الذي به يعلم الشاهد. قلنا : وكذا الرؤية فإنها كما كانت في حق الشاهد ضرورة كانت في حق الغائب كذا. وما ذكر من المسافة والمقابلة واتصال الشعاع قد أبطلنا أن يكون شيء منه من لوازم الرؤية بل كل شيء يرى كما هو وعلى ما هو، فلما كانت الأجسام في الجهات منا وبيننا وبينها قواء رأيناها كما هي وعلى ما هي عليه، لا لأن الجهة والمقابلة من لوازم الرؤية. والله تعالى ليس بمتناه ولا منا بجهة فيرى كما هو يرانا هو لا عن جهة ولا في جهة.
And similarly their statement: 'Knowledge of the unseen does not depart from the manner by which the witnessed is known.' We say: And similarly vision, for just as it is in the case of the witnessed by necessity, it is in the case of the unseen likewise. And what was mentioned of distance, confrontation, and connection of a ray, we have already refuted that any of them is a necessary concomitant of vision; rather, everything is seen as it is and as it is. Since bodies are in directions from us and between us and them there is empty space, we see them as they are and as they are, not because direction and confrontation are necessary concomitants of vision. And God the Exalted is not finite nor in a direction from us, so He is seen as He is; He sees us not from a direction nor in a direction.
Source pages p. 644

Commentary
This passage responds to the claim that knowledge of the unseen must follow the same pattern as knowledge of the witnessed. Nasafi retorts that vision is analogous: just as vision of witnessed things occurs necessarily under certain conditions (here, direction etc.), so too vision of the unseen (God) occurs in its own manner. He then reiterates that distance, confrontation, and ray connection have been proven not to be necessary for vision; rather, every object is seen as it is. Bodies in space are seen in a direction because they exist in space with empty space between, but that is due to their nature, not because direction is a necessary condition for vision itself. God, being infinite and not in a direction, is seen as He is, without direction. He also sees us without direction. Thus the analogy with knowledge supports the claim that God's vision is not conditioned by physical requirements.

Key Terms
العلم بالغائب
الشاهد
المسافة
القواء
الجهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that analogical reasoning about God must be unqualified—that since we see created things only in a direction, we cannot see God at all.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the analogy between knowledge and vision is valid, but the conditions (direction, distance) are not transferable to God because they are not essential to vision itself.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi argue about vision in relation to the analogy with knowledge?
A) Vision of the unseen must follow exactly the same rules as vision of the witnessed.
B) Vision of the unseen is analogous to knowledge of the unseen, not requiring same conditions.
C) Vision is only possible for the witnessed, not the unseen.
D) The analogy fails because knowledge is different from vision.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi defends the vision of God by analogy with knowledge: both do not require the conditions of the created world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you place limitations on how you think God can see you or be seen? Have you ever felt that because you cannot see God now, He is far away? How does this lesson challenge that assumption? What will you do to remind yourself that God sees you even without direction or distance?
?
When you try to know God, do you demand that His attributes conform to your physical experience? This lesson says knowledge of the unseen is not bound by the rules of the witnessed. In your daily life, do you trust God's unseen wisdom even when it contradicts your immediate senses? What area of your life do you need to let go of your own 'sensory' expectations and submit to His unseen plan?
?
The passage refutes that direction and confrontation are necessary for vision. Do you sometimes assume God is 'nearer' when you feel something, or 'farther' when you don't? How can you train yourself to know that God is always 'seen' to you through His signs, regardless of your emotional state? Write down one concrete step to act on that conviction today.

Refuting the Whole and Part Objection
Nasafi refutes the objection that if God is seen, He must be seen partially or wholly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that God must be seen wholly or partially is invalid, and how Nasafi refutes it by showing that 'whole' and 'part' only apply to composites.

Core Idea
Seeing something does not require it to be whole or part — God is seen as He is.

When considering the vision of God, some assume that if God is seen, He must be seen either wholly or partially, implying limitation. Nasafi directly refutes this assumption by showing that the notions of 'whole' and 'part' only apply to composite beings. In this passage, he presents both a dialectical argument and a precise conceptual answer, using the example of accidents to clarify.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument flow before you interpret.
Notice the structure: first a dialectical objection-answer, then the real answer, then an analogy.
2
Understand the dialectical argument
Nasafi counters by asking objectors whether they know God wholly or partially. Explore why each possible answer leads to impossibility or disbelief, except the answer 'we know Him as He is.'
This argument mirrors the same logic for God seeing us.
3
Grasp the real answer
Nasafi defines 'whole' as a composition of limited parts and 'part' as a component. These cannot apply to God, so He is seen as He is, not as whole or part.
Consider how this definition removes the objection.
4
Study the analogy with accidents
Nasafi uses the example of seeing a movement (an accident): an accident is not described as whole or part, yet it is seen. This reinforces that vision does not require compositeness.
The analogy extends to the indivisible atom.
5
Conclude the lesson
Reflect on the key takeaway: God is seen as He is, without whole or part, because these terms only apply to composites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قالوا: لو كان الله تعالى يرى بعضه أو كله على ما قرروا كلام فاسد دفعتهم إليه الحيرة. وأقرب ما يجابون عنه بأن يقابل بالعلم فيقال: أتعلمون الله كله أم بعضه؟ فإن قالوا عرفنا كله أو بعضه أحالوا. وإن قالوا إذا لم نعرف كله ولا بعضه لم نعرفه، كفروا. وإن قالوا نعرفه كما هو وهو ليس بموصوف بالكل والبعض فهو لهم جواب. وكذا يقال: الله تعالى يرانا، أفيرانا بعضه أم كله ؟ والكلام فيه كما في الأول. ثم حقيقة الجواب أن الكل اسم لجملة تركبت من أجزاء محصورة، والبعض اسم لكل جزء وتركب الكل منه ومن غيره، وذلك كله لا يليق بصفات الباري فلم يكن كلا ولا بعضا، فيرى كما هو واعتبر هذا بالعلم به. ثم هذا يبطل، برؤية الأعراض إذ يرى لو أن حركةً، فلو قيل: رأيت كله أم بعضه؟ فبأي الجوابين أجاب أحال، وليس له من الجواب إلا أن يقول: العرض لا يوصف بالكل والبعض لما مر من تحديدهما وذلك محال على العرض. فيرى العرض لا كله ولا بعضه بل يرى على ما هو عليه. فكذا الباري جل وعلا يرى على ما هو عليه لا كله ولا بعضه. وكذا الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ.
And what they said: 'If God the Exalted were seen, either part of Him or all of Him' – as they asserted – is corrupt speech to which confusion drove them. The closest way to answer them is to counter with knowledge and say: Do you know God wholly or partially? If they say we know Him wholly or partially, they fall into impossibility. And if they say: If we do not know Him wholly nor partially, we do not know Him, they disbelieve. And if they say: We know Him as He is, and He is not described by wholeness and partiality, that is an answer for them. And similarly it is said: God the Exalted sees us; does He see us partially or wholly? And the discussion about it is as in the first. Then the real answer is that 'whole' is a name for a composition consisting of limited parts, and 'partial' is a name for each part from which the whole is composed along with others, and all of that is not befitting the attributes of the Creator; so He is not whole nor partial, and He is seen as He is. Consider this in relation to knowledge of Him. Then this is refuted by the vision of accidents, for a movement, if seen, and if it were said: Did you see it wholly or partially? whichever answer he gives he falls into impossibility, and he has no answer except to say: The accident is not described by wholeness and partiality due to what has passed of their definitions, and that is impossible for the accident. So the accident is seen not wholly nor partially, but seen as it is. So likewise the Creator, the Mighty and Exalted, is seen as He is, not wholly nor partially. And likewise the indivisible atom.
Source pages p. 645

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the objection that if God is seen, He must be seen either wholly or partially. Nasafi first presents a dialectical response using knowledge: ask the objector whether they know God wholly or partially. If they say wholly or partially, they imply composition (impossible); if they say they don't know Him at all, they are disbelievers; the only correct answer is that they know Him as He is, without whole/part. The same applies to God's seeing us. Then he gives the real answer: 'whole' and 'part' apply only to composites of limited parts, which does not apply to God. He reinforces this by the example of seeing an accident (like motion): an accident is not described as whole or part, yet it is seen. Thus God, not being a composite, is seen as He is, without whole or part. This also applies to the indivisible atom (al-juz' alladhi la yatajaza').

Key Terms
الكل
البعض
العرض
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ
الرؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God is seen, He must be seen either wholly or partially, implying He is a composite being with parts.
Correction: Vision does not require compositeness. 'Whole' and 'part' only apply to composites, and God is not a composite. He is seen as He is, without being described by whole or part, just as accidents and indivisible atoms are seen without such descriptions.

Checkpoint
What is the 'real answer' (حقيقة الجواب) to the whole/part objection?
A) God is seen wholly because He is all-encompassing.
B) 'Whole' and 'part' apply only to composites, so God is seen without them.
C) God is seen partially because He is incomprehensible.
D) The objection is invalid because we do not see God at all.

Key Takeaway
God is seen as He is, without being described by wholeness or partiality, because these terms only apply to composites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the fact that God is seen 'as He is' and not as 'whole or part' affect your longing to see Him? Are you content to see Him as He is, or do you still imagine Him in limited ways?
?
Nasafi uses the example of seeing an accident to show that vision does not require composition. In your own life, when have you tried to reduce God to your own categories? What would it mean to let go of those categories and know God as He is?
?
The objectors were confused by their own assumptions. What assumptions do you hold about God that might be limiting your understanding of Him? Are you willing to be corrected by the truth, even if it challenges your comfortable concepts?

Framing the Vision of God: Eschatological vs Essential
Nasafi distinguishes between two framings of the vision question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi recommends framing the vision of God debate in terms of essential visibility rather than eschatological occurrence.

Core Idea
Shift the question from 'when' to 'what' — focus on essential visibility to sidestep objections.

Nasafi notes that many standard objections to the vision of God apply only to those who affirm it in the afterlife. He recommends reframing the discussion: ask whether God's essence is intrinsically visible, not when it will occur. This methodological shift avoids irrelevant objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to absorb the structure.
Notice the contrast between 'من يقول' and 'من فرض الكلام'.
2
Identify the two framings
The passage presents two ways to discuss divine vision: eschatological (seen in afterlife) and essential (visible in itself). Find the Arabic phrases for each.
3
Understand the methodological advice
The Samarqand scholars advised their companions to adopt the essential framing. Ask: why does this avoid most objections?
The commentary explains that objections like the whole/part dilemma apply only to the eschatological view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أكثر هذه الأسئلة ترد على من يقول : يرى الله تعالى في الآخرة. فأما من فرض الكلام في أن ذاته هل هو مرئي في نفسه فيسقط عنه أكثر هذه الأسئلة. وبعض الكبراء المحققين من أئمتنا بسمرقند كان يوصي أصحابنا أن يفرضوا
Then most of these questions are directed at one who says: God the Exalted is seen in the afterlife. But as for one who frames the discussion as to whether His essence is visible in itself, most of these questions fall away from him. And some of the great verifying scholars among our imams in Samarqand used to advise our companions to posit...
Source pages p. 645

Commentary
This passage notes that most of the preceding objections (like the whole/part dilemma) apply to those who affirm that God will be seen specifically in the afterlife (الآخرة). However, if one shifts the discussion to the essential visibility of God's essence — i.e., whether His essence is intrinsically capable of being seen (مرئي في نفسه) — then many of those objections become irrelevant. The passage then mentions that some senior verifying scholars among the Maturidi imams in Samarqand used to advise their companions to posit (probably to posit the discussion of essential visibility). The sentence is cut off, but the intent is to recommend framing the question in terms of essential visibility rather than eschatological occurrence to avoid unnecessary objections.

Key Terms
الآخرة
مرئي في نفسه
الكبراء المحققين
سمرقند

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Confusing the theological question of whether God can be seen in principle (essential possibility) with the eschatological fact that He will be seen.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies they are distinct and recommends focusing on the essential framing to avoid irrelevant objections.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, for whom do most of the objections about seeing God fall away?
A) Those who affirm God is seen in the afterlife.
B) Those who deny God can be seen at all.
C) Those who discuss whether God's essence is visible in itself.
D) Those who say God is seen partially.

Key Takeaway
The way you frame the theological question determines which objections apply; focusing on essential visibility avoids many irrelevant issues.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you were stuck in a theological or religious discussion, weighed down by endless objections. Could it be that the way you framed the question was causing those objections? How might shifting the framing, as Nasafi advises, have changed the conversation? Will you commit to examining the framing before diving into debate?
?
This passage shows that senior scholars intentionally chose to focus on essential visibility rather than eschatological details. In your own study of God's attributes, do you find yourself more drawn to concrete timelines (when?) or to logical possibilities (how?)? Which approach is more likely to bring you closer to certainty? What will you change today?
?
The scholar's advice was to 'posit' the essential framing to avoid unnecessary objections. What current doubts or criticisms about God's attributes have been shaped by your own framing rather than by the reality of God? Are you willing to let go of a comfortable but problematic framing to see the issue more clearly?

Answering the Whole-or-Part Objection by Analogy with Knowledge
Al-Nasafi responds to the whole-or-part objection using knowledge as an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Nasafi refutes the objection to seeing God by turning the dilemma back onto knowledge of God.

Core Idea
The same dilemma used against seeing God also applies to knowing God — and the answer is 'as He is.'

Opponents of the vision of God argue that seeing must involve either the whole or a part of God, both of which are impossible for the Divine. Al-Nasafi cleverly responds by asking whether they know God entirely or partially, exposing the same logical trap. This passage shows how a theological objection can be neutralised by reverse analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Note the structure: objection, counter-question, three possible answers, and extension to God's seeing.
The passage is a continuous argument.
2
Identify the three possible responses
Al-Nasafi presents three possible answers to the question about knowledge of God. List them and note the consequence of each.
The third response is the correct one.
3
Connect the analogy
Understand how the same reasoning applied to knowledge also applies to the original objection about seeing. See why the objection is self-defeating.
This shows the power of logical refutation in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قالوا: لو كان الله تعالى يرى بعضه أو كله على ما قرروا كلام فاسد دفعتهم إليه الحيرة. وأقرب ما يجابون عنه بأن يقابل بالعلم فيقال: أتعلمون الله كله أم بعضه؟ فإن قالوا عرفنا كله أو بعضه أحالوا. وإن قالوا إذا لم نعرف كله ولا بعضه لم نعرفه، كفروا. وإن قالوا نعرفه كما هو وهو ليس بموصوف بالكل والبعض فهو لهم جواب. وكذا يقال: الله تعالى يرانا، أفيرانا بعضه أم كله؟ والكلام فيه كما في الأول.
And what they said: 'If God the Exalted sees some of Him or all of Him' according to what they established is corrupt speech to which confusion drives them. And the closest way to answer them is to counter with knowledge and say: Do you know God entirely or partially? If they say we know Him entirely or partially they fall into impossibility. If they say we do not know Him entirely nor partially and we do not know Him, they disbelieve. If they say we know Him as He is, and He is not described by whole or part, that is for them an answer. And likewise it is said: God the Exalted sees us—does He see some of us or all of us? And the discussion concerning it is as in the first.
Source pages p. 645

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection against the possibility of seeing God in the hereafter. The opponents argue that if God is seen, one must see either all of Him or a part of Him, both of which are impossible for God. Al-Nasafi responds by turning the argument around: he asks whether they know God entirely or partially. The same dilemma applies: claiming to know God entirely or partially is impossible, but denying knowledge of Him entirely or partially leads to disbelief. The only consistent answer is that they know God as He is, without being described by 'whole' or 'part.' This answer also applies to the claim that God sees us: we do not say God sees all of us or part of us, but that He sees us as we are.

Key Terms
رؤية
كل
بعض
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That seeing God must involve seeing either the whole or a part of God, making it impossible.
Correction: The same logic applies to knowing God, and the correct answer is that we know Him as He is, not by whole or part.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the closest way to answer those who object that seeing God implies whole or part?
A) By denying that God can be seen at all.
B) By countering with the same question about knowledge of God.
C) By stating that God is composed of parts.
D) By saying that God sees only part of Himself.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi refutes the objection to seeing God by showing that the same dilemma applies to knowledge of God, and the only consistent answer is to know God as He is.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you try to know God, do you ever feel trapped by human categories like 'whole' or 'part'? How does al-Nasafi's argument free you from that mental cage? Commit today to knowing God as He is, not as your mind tries to box Him in.
?
If someone asked you, 'Do you know God entirely or partially?', what would you honestly answer? Does your answer reveal a lack of trust in the knowledge He has given you? Resolve to embrace knowing Him through His attributes without demanding to encompass His essence.
?
Al-Nasafi warns that denying knowledge of God leads to disbelief. Have you ever avoided deeper reflection on God because you felt overwhelmed or inadequate? This lesson calls you to press in—how will you intentionally seek to know Him more this week?

Divine Attributes

Whole and Part Do Not Apply to God
Al-Nasafi explains the true answer regarding whole/part and God's vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the terms 'whole' and 'part' cannot be used for God, and how Al-Nasafi uses the vision of accidents to demonstrate this principle.

Core Idea
God is not composed, so whole and part are inapplicable to Him.

Al-Nasafi now gives the definitive answer to the objection that seeing God would require seeing Him as whole or part. He defines these terms and shows they only apply to composite things. Then he proves his point by considering how we see accidents—like motion—without applying whole/part. This establishes that God is seen as He is, without division.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definitions
Read the passage's definitions of 'whole' and 'part'. Note that these terms apply only to composed things.
'Whole' is a totality of limited parts; 'part' is a component.
2
See the analogy with accidents
Al-Nasafi uses the example of seeing a motion (an accident). If asked whether you saw all of it or part of it, both answers are impossible. This shows that some things are seen without whole/part.
3
Apply to God and the indivisible atom
Conclude that God is seen as He is, neither whole nor part. The indivisible atom (al-juz' alladhi la yatajzza') also avoids these categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم حقيقة الجواب أن الكل اسم لجملة تركبت من أجزاء محصورة، والبعض اسم لكل جزء وتركب الكل منه ومن غيره، وذلك كله لا يليق بصفات الباري فلم يكن كلا ولا بعضا، فيرى كما هو واعتبر هذا بالعلم به. ثم هذا يبطل، برؤية الأعراض إذ يرى لو أن حركة، فلو قيل: رأيت كله أم بعضه؟ فبأي الجوابين أجاب أحال، وليس له من الجواب إلا أن يقول: العرض يوصف بالكل والبعض لما مر من تحديدهما وذلك محال على العرض. فيرى العرض لا كله ولا بعضه بل يرى على ما هو عليه. فكذا الباري جل وعلا يرى على ما هو عليه لا كله ولا بعضه. وكذا الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ.
Then the true answer is that 'whole' is a name for a totality composed of limited parts, and 'part' is a name for each part from which the whole and others are composed. All of that is not fitting for the attributes of the Creator, so He is neither whole nor part. He is seen as He is. And consider this by analogy with knowledge of Him. Then this is invalidated by the vision of accidents, since one sees, for example, a movement. If it were said: Did you see all of it or part of it? Whichever of the two answers one gives leads to impossibility. He has no answer except to say: The accident is described by whole and part due to what has passed of their definitions, and that is impossible for the accident. So one sees the accident neither as whole nor part, but sees it as it is. Likewise the Exalted and Glorious Creator is seen as He is, not whole nor part. And likewise the indivisible atom.
Source pages p. 645

Commentary
This passage gives the true resolution of the whole-or-part objection. 'Whole' and 'part' are terms that apply only to composite things. God is not composite, so these terms do not apply to Him—He is seen as He is. Al-Nasafi then proves this by considering the vision of an accident, like motion. If one asks whether one saw all of the motion or part of it, both answers are impossible because an accident is not composed in that way. The only proper answer is that one sees it as it is, not as whole or part. Similarly, the indivisible atom (al-juz' alladhi la yatajzza') is seen without being described by whole or part. Therefore, God is seen as He is, without division.

Key Terms
كل
بعض
عرض
جزء لا يتجزأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Whenever something is seen, it must be seen as either whole or part.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that accidents and the indivisible atom are seen without being described as whole or part, so God is likewise.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that 'whole' and 'part' are inapplicable to God?
A) Because God is too great to be seen.
B) Because God is not composed of parts.
C) Because the terms are only used for bodies.
D) Because the question is forbidden to ask.

Key Takeaway
Whole and part are terms for composition; God has no composition, so He is seen as He is, not as whole or part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God, do you unintentionally imagine Him as having parts or limits? How does this passage correct that tendency, and what must you change in your mental image?
?
If you were asked to describe what you 'see' of God with your heart, would you be tempted to divide His attributes? Why is that a subtle form of shirk, and how can you train your mind to see Him as He is?
?
Al-Nasafi uses the example of seeing a motion—something you experience daily. How often do you think about the limitations of your perception? What does this teach you about trusting your intellect versus revelation when it comes to understanding God?

Answering Why God Is Not Seen Now Despite No Veil
Al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite objection using a dilemma and analogical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite argument that the present non-vision of God proves He cannot be seen, using a dilemma and analogical reasoning about the nature of obstacles to vision.

Core Idea
Non-vision now does not prove impossibility; the obstacle is in our capacity, not in God.

The Mu'tazilites argue that since we do not see God now despite there being no veil or obstacle, this proves that seeing God is impossible. Al-Nasafi responds with a clever dilemma: either the obstacle is an opposite (blindness) or external factors. He then shows that both horns lead to conclusions the Mu'tazilites cannot accept, and further argues that God can strengthen our sight, so non-vision now is due to our weakness, not God's invisibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite objection
Start by reading the opening lines where the objection is presented: why is God not seen now despite no veil? This is the challenge al-Nasafi will refute.
Notice that the objection assumes that if something is visible, it would be seen now.
2
Follow the dilemma: what is the obstacle?
Al-Nasafi presents two possible explanations for the absence of vision: either the obstacle is an opposite (like blindness) residing in the viewer, or it is external factors like veils, smallness, or distance. Identify these two horns.
This is the key structuring move of the argument.
3
Examine the first horn: opposition in the locus
If the Mu'tazilites choose that the obstacle is an opposite (i.e., blindness), then they admit that vision is only prevented by an opposite. Since there is no blindness in the believers now, God could be seen. Thus they have conceded the point.
Al-Nasafi says this cuts off their clamor.
4
Examine the second horn: external factors and implications
If they choose external factors, then vision can cease without an opposite. This leads to a further argument: if vision can cease without an opposite, then other accidents like motion and color can also cease without an opposite—something the Mu'tazilites deny. Thus this horn is also problematic.
This is an analogical reduction.
5
Consider the capacity argument
Al-Nasafi then argues that God can strengthen our eyesight to see small and distant objects. Therefore minuteness is not an absolute barrier to vision—it sometimes accompanies vision and sometimes not. Hence the current non-vision is due to our limited strength, not impossibility.
This shows that the cause of non-vision is not intrinsic to the object.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد خرج الجواب عما قالوا: إنا لماذا لا نرى الله في الحال ولا حجب ولا موانع؟ فإنا بينا قبل ذلك ما هو جوابه على الاستقصاء، ثم نزيد لهذا إيضاحا لأن المعتزلة يتشبثون به ويوهمون الأحداث أنه من قبيل ما لا انفصال عنه. فنقول لهم: وجود الحجب والسواتر ودقة المرئي وبعده ما المانع من الرؤية أقيام ضدها بمحلها أم الحجب والسواتر والدقة واللطافة والبعد؟ فإن قالوا بالأول، فقد أذعنوا للحق وانقطع شغبهم، وإن قالوا بالثاني وأضافوا ارتفاع الرؤية إلى هذه الأشياء وإن لم تكن قائمة بمحل الرؤية. قيل لهم: إذا جاز أن تنعدم الرؤية للشيء لا إلى ضدها فلم لا يجوز ذلك في الأعراض حتى يخلو الجسم عن الحركة لا إلى ضدها ومن اللون لا إلى ضد له. ثم يقال لهم: أليس قد يجوز أن يقوى الله تعالى أبصارنا فنرى الجسم الصغير والدقيق والبعيد؟ فإذا قالوا نعم. قيل لهم: فما أنكرتم من أن تكون الدقة التي تجامع الرؤية تارة ولا تجامعها تارة لا تكون علة لأن يكون الشيء غير مرئي كما لا تكون علة لأن يكون مرئيا إذ قد تجامع الرؤية تارة ولا تجامعها تارة. ويقال لهم: ما أنكرتم أن يكون ما يجامع الرؤية تارة ولا يجامعها تارة لا يكون علة لأن كان الشيء مرئيا كما أن وجود الجسم الذي يجامع الحركة تارة ولا يجامعها تارة لا يكون علة لأن كان الشيء متحركا. وكذا لا يكون علة لئلا يكون مرئيا لوجود العلة في
And the answer has emerged to what they said: 'Why do we not see God at present, while there is no veil nor obstacle?' For we have already explained before its answer in detail. Then we add clarification to this because the Mu'tazilites cling to it and make the common people imagine that it is of the kind from which there is no escape. So we say to them: The existence of veils, screens, minuteness of the seen object, and its distance—what is the obstacle to vision? Is it the existence of its opposite in its locus, or are the veils, screens, minuteness, subtlety, and distance? If they say the first, they have acknowledged the truth and their clamor is cut off. If they say the second and attribute the cessation of vision to these things even though they are not subsisting in the locus of vision, it is said to them: If it is permissible for vision of a thing to cease without [arising from] its opposite, then why is that not permissible for accidents, so that a body becomes devoid of motion without an opposite, and of color without an opposite? Then it is said to them: Is it not possible that God the Exalted strengthens our eyesight so that we see a small, minute, and distant body? If they say yes, it is said to them: What prevents you from [accepting] that minuteness, which sometimes accompanies vision and sometimes does not accompany it, is not a cause for a thing being unseen, just as it is not a cause for it being seen, since it sometimes accompanies vision and sometimes does not? And it is said to them: What prevents you from [accepting] that what accompanies vision sometimes and does not accompany it sometimes is not a cause for a thing being visible, just as the existence of a body, which accompanies motion sometimes and does not accompany it sometimes, is not a cause for a thing being in motion? And likewise it is not a cause for it being unseen, because of the existence of the cause in
Source pages p. 646

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed refutation of a Mu'tazilite objection: why is God not seen now if there is no veil or obstacle? The author says he has already answered this before but adds further clarity. He presents a two-part dilemma: the obstacle to vision is either the existence of an opposite in the locus of vision (i.e., blindness) or external factors like veils, distance, etc. If they say the first, they admit that vision is only prevented by an opposite, which is not currently the case, so God could be seen. If they say the second, then vision can cease without an opposite—and the author uses this to argue by analogy: if vision can cease without an opposite, then other things like motion and color can also cease without an opposite, which the Mu'tazilites deny. Then he argues that God can strengthen our eyesight so that we see small and distant objects. If they accept that, then minuteness is not an absolute barrier to vision—it sometimes accompanies vision, sometimes not—so it cannot be a cause for something being unseen. Similarly, bodily constitution accompanies motion contingently, not as a cause. The argument is cut off but aims to show that the absence of vision now does not imply impossibility.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that the absence of vision now proves impossibility?

B) By showing that vision can cease without an opposite and that God can strengthen sight.
C) By accepting that veils are necessary for vision.
D) By denying that God could be seen even in the hereafter.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite objection by showing that the absence of vision now does not prove impossibility, because the obstacle could be our weakness, not God's nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel spiritually distant and wonder why God does not make His presence more obvious to you, do you tend to blame Him for hiding or yourself for not being ready? How does Al-Nasafi's argument challenge you to take responsibility for cultivating the spiritual eyesight to see God's signs now, and to long for the ultimate vision in the hereafter?
?
The Mu'tazilites demanded immediate evidence. Have you ever caught yourself demanding that God prove Himself to you on your terms, rather than trusting His wisdom in delaying full disclosure? What would it mean for you to accept that your current inability to see God is a reflection of your own limitation, not His deficiency, and to humbly ask Him to strengthen your faith?
?
Al-Nasafi notes that the same minuteness sometimes allows vision and sometimes blocks it, depending on the strength of the sight. What are the 'minute' distractions in your life—small sins or worldly attachments—that sometimes cloud your perception of God and sometimes don't? What concrete step will you take today to remove a particular barrier that you have been tolerating?

The Initial Challenge: Why Do We Not See God Now?
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazila objection and the first bifurcation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's objection that God should be visible now and the first key distinction between causes of non-vision (opposite vs external factors).

Core Idea
Non-vision is either due to its opposite or external factors—neither supports the Mu'tazila.

The Mu'tazila argue that since there are no veils or obstacles, we should see God now. Imam Nasafi begins his refutation by analyzing the possible causes for the absence of vision. He presents a crucial bifurcation that will undermine their entire case.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Focus on the question and the two possible answers given by Nasafi.
The passage is dense; do not rush.
2
Identify the bifurcation
Find the two possibilities Nasafi presents for why vision is absent: either because of an opposite in the locus, or because of external factors like veils, smallness, distance.
This is the core logical move.
3
Understand the implications
Consider what follows if the Mu'tazila choose either option. If they choose 'opposite', they concede the principle; if they choose 'external', the next arguments in later lessons will show inconsistency.
This sets the stage for the entire refutation.

Tabsirat al-Adilla of Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, vol. 1. Discourse on proving that He is visible in His essence to repulse most of these questions. And we have given the answer to what they said: Why do we not see God now, without veils or obstacles? For we explained before what its answer is in detail, then we add clarification to this because the Mu'tazila cling to it and make the common people think (1) that it is of the kind from which there is no separation. So we say to them: The existence of veils, screens, smallness of the seen object, and its distance—what prevents vision? Is it the existence of its opposite in its locus, or the veils, screens, smallness, subtlety, and distance? If they say the first, they have submitted to the truth and their clamor ceases. If they say the second and attribute the absence of vision to these things even though they are not subsisting in the locus of vision—all of them—it is said to them: If it is permissible that vision ceases for a thing without its opposite, then why is that not permissible for accidents so that the body becomes devoid of motion without its opposite, and of color without its opposite? Then it is said to them: Is it not possible that Allah Almighty strengthens our sight so that we see the small, minute, and distant body? If they say yes, it is said to them: What prevents you from [accepting] that the smallness which accompanies vision sometimes and does not accompany it other times is not a cause for the thing to be unseen, just as it is not a cause for it to be seen, since (3) it may accompany vision sometimes and not accompany it other times. And it is said to them: What prevents you from [accepting] that what accompanies vision sometimes and does not accompany it other times is not a cause for the thing to be seen, just as the existence (4) of the body which accompanies motion sometimes and does not accompany it other times is not a cause for the thing to be moving. Similarly, it is not a cause for it not to be seen because the cause exists in both states and there is no effect. And it is said: How does the vision of the minute and distant cease from the eye without an opposite that makes the locus of vision [reside] in the eye of the seer and the absence reside in another? And how is it permissible that a thing ceases due to the existence of an accident in something other than its locus? Then it is said to them (1): Why do you claim that what is possible to be seen, when we do not see it, (we do not see it only)(2) due to the defects (3) that you enumerated: smallness, veil, absence, distance, and subtlety? If they claim that what is between us is like that, it is said to them: Why do you claim that what is between us is like that, despite our disagreement with you regarding what you have claimed, and our denial that any of that is unseen as you mentioned in the witnessed world, and our assertion that the cessation of vision is due to the existence of the opposite of vision? Then it is said to them: Your opinion always reverts to mere existence and blindness to the realities of causes. And why did you judge according to that when you found it so? Then they are required to judge that the thing in the unseen is not except substance or accident, and that which subsists by itself is not except substance (4) because they found it so. Then, from their foolishness, they found in the witnessed world every knower described with knowledge, yet they did not judge accordingly in the unseen; and although knowledge is related to the knower as causes are related to effects, they judged in the unseen according to what is conjoined with another in the witnessed world by conjunction of existence. And this, its corruption is not hidden. And when one of the disciples of al-Jubba'i saw this implication, he claimed that the smallness of the body prevents vision because of weakness in sight (5). So it is said to them: Is it possible that weakness disappears while smallness exists? If they say no, they have made an impossibility in their speech, because the subsistence of a meaning in a locus does not prevent the existence of
Source pages p. 646

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Mu'tazila objections regarding the impossibility of seeing God (ru'ya) in this world. The Imam explains that he has already given a detailed answer but adds clarification because the Mu'tazila mislead common people by claiming that the impossibility of seeing God now is an inherent impossibility. He then presents a series of dialectical arguments. First, he asks the Mu'tazila: what prevents vision now? Is it the existence of an opposite (like blindness) in the locus of vision, or is it external factors like veils, smallness, distance? If they admit it is due to an opposite, they concede the principle that absence of vision requires an opposite, which would then apply in the case of God (who has no opposite), thus making ru'ya possible if He wills. If they say it is due to external factors, then the Imam argues that if vision can cease without an opposite, why cannot accidents like motion and color cease without their opposites? This shows inconsistency. Next, he questions whether Allah can strengthen our sight to see small and distant objects. If they agree, then smallness is not a permanent cause of invisibility because it sometimes accompanies vision (when sight is strong) and sometimes not. Thus, smallness is not a true cause (ʿilla) of invisibility. Similarly, he draws an analogy: the existence of a body sometimes accompanies motion and sometimes not, so it is not a cause of motion; likewise, external factors are not causes of invisibility. He then asks: how can vision of a minute or distant object be negated by an accident (like smallness) that does not reside in the eye itself? This is impossible because effects require their causes to be in the same locus. Then he challenges their claim that we do not see God only due to obstacles like smallness, veil, etc. But the Imam denies that these are true obstacles based on the witnessed world (shāhid), and asserts that the true cause of non-vision is the opposite of vision (i.e., blindness). He criticizes the Mu'tazila for inconsistently applying analogical reasoning (qiyās): they sometimes base judgments on mere coexistence in the witnessed world without establishing causality, and other times they impose strict categories (e.g., that the unseen must be either substance or accident) based on witness. But then they fail to apply the same reasoning to attributes like knowledge: in witness, every knower has knowledge, yet they do not affirm knowledge for God in the unseen, even though knowledge relates to the knower as cause to effect. This shows their methodology is flawed. Finally, he addresses a specific argument from a follower of al-Jubba'i who claimed that a body's smallness prevents vision due to weakness of sight. The Imam counters: if weakness can disappear while smallness remains, then smallness alone is not a preventer. If they say weakness cannot disappear with smallness, they contradict themselves because a meaning (maʿnā) in a locus does not preclude the existence of another meaning, so the two can coexist.

Key Terms
رؤية
ضد
حجاب
دقة
بعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that since we do not see God now, it is impossible to see Him at all.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the absence of vision now can be explained by external factors (which are not permanent) or by an opposite (which does not apply to God). Neither implies impossibility.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Nasafi's first argument, what are the two possible causes for not seeing something?
A) The existence of an opposite in the locus of vision, or external factors like veils and smallness.
B) The weakness of the seer's sight, or the absence of light.
C) The object being too small, or the seer being distant.
D) The will of God, or the sinfulness of the seer.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila are forced to admit that non-vision is due to an opposite (which does not apply to God) or external factors (which are not permanent causes).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel spiritually blind or distant from God, do you immediately assume that God is unreachable, or do you honestly ask yourself whether it is your own inner opposite (your sins, your heedlessness) or external veils (distractions, attachments) that are blocking His presence? Will you take one concrete step today to remove one of those veils?
?
The Mu'tazila used the fact that we don't see God now to deny the possibility of seeing Him in the afterlife. Have you ever allowed a temporary spiritual emptiness to make you doubt an eternal promise? How does Nasafi's logical bifurcation help you stand firm when you cannot feel God?
?
You have read that non-vision can be due to an opposite within you or external factors. Which one do you think is the greater obstacle in your own life: your internal state (like pride or laziness) or external circumstances (like busyness or bad company)? Decide right now: what will you do to address that obstacle starting this week?

Accidents and the Inconsistency of Causes
Nasafi argues that non-vision without opposite leads to absurdity with accidents, and smallness is not a true cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that if vision can cease without an opposite, then accidents like motion should also be able to cease without opposites, and that external factors are not true causes.

Core Idea
External factors like smallness are not consistent causes—they accompany vision sometimes, not always.

Nasafi now turns to the second branch. He shows that if the Mu'tazila claim vision ceases without an opposite, then why not also allow accidents like motion to cease without their opposites? He then argues that smallness and other factors cannot be true causes because they sometimes coexist with vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic translation noting the two sub-arguments: the 'accident' analogy and the 'smallness' argument with strengthened sight.
Pay attention to the word 'علة' (cause) and the analogies.
2
Analyze the accident analogy
Nasafi draws a parallel: if vision can end without its opposite (e.g., blindness), then why can't motion end without its opposite (rest)? This exposes inconsistency in Mu'tazila logic.
3
Understand the smallness argument
Nasafi asks whether Allah can strengthen sight so we see small objects. If yes, then smallness is not a permanent preventer—it is not a true cause of invisibility.
