Two Positions on Seeing God's Attributes
Presents two positions on seeing God's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two scholarly positions on the permissibility of seeing God's attributes and their underlying principles.

Core Idea
Whether attributes are seeable depends on whether vision attaches to essences or to any existent.

In the previous passage, the author discussed the vision of God's essence. Now he turns to His attributes, noting that there is no revealed proof for seeing them, but the rational permissibility is debated among early scholars. This passage presents two opinions from the early masters of the school.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice how the author distinguishes between the two opinions.
The passage begins by referencing the earlier discussion on the essence.
2
Identify the two positions
Find the two positions: one that restricts vision to what is self-subsistent, and one that allows vision for every existent. Note the key terms 'qa'im bi al-dhat' and 'mawjud'.
These positions come from the 'qadama'ina' (early scholars) of the Maturidi school.
3
Reflect on the role of revealed proof
The author states there is no revealed proof (dalil sam'i) for seeing the attributes. Ask yourself: does the lack of revealed proof mean it is impossible, or just that it is not established?
The passage only discusses rational permissibility (jawaz), not actual occurrence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا الذي بينا هو الكلام في إثبات رؤية ذاته. فأما صفاته تعالى فلم يرد دليل سمعي برؤيتها، فأما جواز رؤيتها هل هو ثابت؟ فعلى قول قدمائنا القائلين بجواز تعلق الرؤية بالقائم بالذات واستحالة تعلقها بما ليس بقائم بالذات لم تكن صفات الله تعالى جائزة الرؤية. وأما على قول القائلين بجواز تعلق الرؤية بكل موجود فهي جائزة الرؤية.
This that we have explained is the discourse on establishing the vision of His essence. As for His attributes, there has come no revealed proof (dalil sam'i) for seeing them. As for the permissibility of seeing them, is it established? According to the statement of our predecessors who say that it is permissible for vision to attach to what is self-subsistent (al-qa'im bi al-dhat) and impossible for it to attach to what is not self-subsistent, then the attributes of God are not permissible to be seen. And according to the statement of those who say that it is permissible for vision to attach to every existent (mawjud), then they are permissible to be seen.
Source pages p. 657

Commentary
This passage is a brief presentation of two positions on the permissibility of seeing God's attributes. It begins by summarizing the previous discussion on the vision of God's essence (dhat). Then it notes that there is no revealed proof (dalil sam'i) for seeing the attributes. The author then asks whether it is rationally permissible (ja'iz) to see them. He presents two opinions from the early scholars (qadama'ina): the first holds that vision can only attach to what is self-subsistent (al-qa'im bi al-dhat), i.e., an essence, and cannot attach to what does not subsist by itself, like attributes; therefore, attributes are not permissible to be seen. The second opinion holds that vision can attach to any existent (mawjud), regardless of whether it subsists by itself or not; accordingly, attributes are permissible to be seen. The author does not prefer one view here, but simply states the two positions.

Correction: The debate is not about physicality but about whether vision can attach to non-self-subsistent entities. The two positions differ on the scope of vision, not on the physical nature of attributes.

Key Takeaway

?

The Proof for Seeing God: Vision Attaches to Existence
Summarises the proof that vision attaches to existence, extended to the unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical method used to prove that vision attaches to existence itself, thereby establishing the possibility of seeing God.

Core Idea
Vision attaches to existence, not to physical conditions like distance or direction.

This passage provides the summary of a key theological argument for the possibility of seeing God. Theologians observed that vision applies to many different visible objects; the only common feature is existence. Therefore, vision is attached to existence. This principle is then extended to the unseen realm, showing that God, who exists, can be seen. Additionally, God's vision of us in this life shows that accidental conditions are not necessary for vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the logical flow from observation to conclusion.
The Arabic text is slightly truncated; focus on the argument structure.
2
Identify the key move: common attribute
The passage states that among all visible things, the only common attribute is existence. Understand why this is crucial for the argument.
This is like a logical induction: from many instances, abstract the universal.
3
Understand the extension to the unseen
The argument extends the conclusion about vision to the unseen world (gha'ib) by transferring the common attribute of existence. This is a standard method in kalam.
The extension relies on the principle that what applies to the witnessed applies to the unseen if the cause is the same.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this argument opens the door to the vision of God in the afterlife. Also note that it counters misconceptions that vision requires physical proximity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل في المسألة أنا لما رأينا الرؤية متعلقة بتلك الأجناس، ولا وصف يجمعها يمكن تعلقها به إلا الوجود علقناها به لوجود دلیل تعلقها به تعلق الأحكام بالعلل وعديناها إلى الغائب بتعدي الوجود، ودلتنا رؤية الباري جل وعلا إيانا على أن ما اقترن بالرؤية في الشاهد من المباينة والمقابلة واتصال الشعاع وغير
And the conclusion (al-hasil) of the issue is that when we see that vision (al-ru'ya) is attached to those kinds [of visible things], and there is no description that encompasses them that vision can attach to except existence (al-wujud), we attach it to existence because of the existence of a proof that it attaches to it as judgments (al-ahkam) attach to causes (al-'ilal), and we extend it to the unseen (al-gha'ib) by the extension of existence. And the vision of the Creator, glorified and exalted, of us indicates that what is associated with vision in the witnessed world (al-shahid) such as separation (al-mubayana), facing (al-muqabala), and the connection of rays (ittisal al-shu'a'), and other [conditions]...
Source pages p. 657

Commentary
This passage is the summary of the reasoning for the possibility of seeing God. It explains the method used by theologians: in the witnessed world (al-shahid), we observe that vision attaches to various objects (different kinds). The only common attribute among all those visible objects is existence (al-wujud). Therefore, we conclude that vision attaches to existence itself, in the way that judgments attach to their causes. This is then extended to the unseen (al-gha'ib) by analogy through the shared attribute of existence. Additionally, the fact that God sees us (ru'ya al-bari ina) indicates that the conditions we normally associate with vision in the created world—such as physical separation (being opposite), facing each other, or the connection of rays of light—are not essential to vision itself. The text is incomplete but the core argument is clear: vision is grounded in existence, which God possesses, so seeing God is rationally possible.

Key Terms

الوجود
الشاهد
الغائب
المباينة
المقابلة
اتصال الشعاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vision requires physical proximity, facing, or rays of light, so God cannot be seen.
Correction: The passage shows that vision is attached to existence, and accidental conditions like facing are not essential. God's own vision of us proves this.

Checkpoint
According to the summary, what is the common attribute among visible objects that allows vision to attach to them?
A) Being a body (jism).
B) Existence (al-wujud).
C) Being an accident (arad).
D) Being in a particular direction.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of seeing God is established by identifying existence as the sole essential condition for vision, which God possesses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If your certainty is merely intellectual, what steps must you take today to transform this knowledge into heartfelt expectation?
?
The proof hinges on God seeing you. He sees you now, without any physical conditions. How does that awareness change the way you live this moment? Are you comfortable being fully seen by your Creator, or do you hide parts of your life from His vision?
?
The argument extends from the witnessed world to the unseen. But you live between both worlds. Does your faith in the unseen—the promise of seeing God—reshape your priorities here and now? What would you need to leave behind to make that vision your ultimate goal?

Negating Limits as Perfection: The Criterion of Contingency
Al-Nasafi summarizes the argument that negating limits is a perfection only when no other defect is present.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that negating signs of contingency (like limits) is a sign of perfection only when no other evidence of contingency remains, and how this applies to the proof of seeing Allah's essence.

Core Idea
Negating limits is perfection only when no other defects remain.

Al-Nasafi concludes his discussion on proving the vision of Allah's essence by addressing a subtle point: simply negating limits is not automatically a sign of perfection. He clarifies that the negation must be accompanied by the absence of any other indicators of contingency. This passage refines the method of establishing divine attributes through negation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then the English translation. Notice the conditional statement: 'if no other evidence is associated with the negated thing.'
2
Identify the two methods of proof
Al-Nasafi mentions two ways: affirming attributes of perfection or negating signs of deficiency. Identify which one is the focus of this passage.
The focus is on negation, but note the overall framework.
3
Analyze the objection and its resolution
The passage addresses why the negation of limits does not confer perfection on accidents, but does so on Allah. Also note the distinction regarding substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بينا أنه يثبت إما بإثبات صفات الكمال وإما بنفي سمات النقص، وفي الجملة على الرؤية لا يحصل لا هذا ولا ذاك مع أن الإدراك ليس برؤية وفي انتفاء الرؤية لا يحصل لما بينا، فأما بنفيه مع ثبوت الرؤية فيحصل التمدح بنفي الحدود والأطراف التي هي من أمارات الحدوث، ونفي أمارات الحدوث من دلائل الكمال إذا لم يقترن بالمنفي عنه دليل آخر من الدلائل، وفي الأعراض اقترنت بها دلائل وأمارات أخر، وكذا الكلام في الجوهر، على أن الجوهر له حدود ونهايات وجهات فلا يرد هذا الإشكال، والله الموفق. هذا الذي بينا هو الكلام في إثبات رؤية ذاته.
We have explained that it is established either by affirming attributes of perfection or by negating signs of deficiency. In summary, with vision, neither this nor that is obtained, even though perception is not vision, and in the absence of vision it is not obtained as we have explained. As for negating them while vision is established, then praise is obtained by negating the limits and extremities that are signs of contingency. And negating signs of contingency is among the evidences of perfection if no other evidence is associated with the negated thing. In the case of accidents, other evidences and signs are associated with them, and likewise the discussion regarding substance, except that substance has limits, ends, and directions, so this objection does not apply. And Allah is the Grantor of success. This that we have explained is the discussion regarding the proof of seeing His essence.
Source pages p. 657

Commentary
This passage summarizes and defends the proof for the possibility of seeing Allah's essence. It states that the establishment of the vision is either by affirming perfect attributes or by negating defects. The author then addresses an objection: In the case of vision, neither affirmation nor negation by itself is sufficient, but the negation of limits becomes a perfection when vision is assumed. He explains that negating signs of contingency is a sign of perfection only if no other evidence of contingency is present. In the case of accidents, such signs are present, so the negation is not a perfection. For substance, it has limits etc., so the objection does not apply. The passage concludes that this is the discussion on proving the vision of the essence.

Key Terms

صفات الكمال
سمات النقص
أمارات الحدوث
عرض
جوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negating limits always indicates perfection regardless of context.
Correction: Al-Nasafi explains that negating limits only indicates perfection when no other signs of contingency are present, as is the case for Allah but not for accidents.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, why is the negation of limits not a sign of perfection in the case of accidents?
A) Because accidents have no limits
B) Because other signs of contingency accompany them
C) Because accidents are not real
D) Because vision does not apply to accidents

Key Takeaway
Negating limits is only a perfection when it is not accompanied by other signs of contingency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What specific hidden defects might you be neglecting?
?
Consider a character trait you often criticize in others. Before judging, examine yourself: do you possess that same trait in a different form? The passage teaches that negating one sign of deficiency does not make something perfect if other signs are present. How does this challenge you to be more honest about your own imperfections before seeking to fix others?
?
When you make istighfar and seek to turn back to Allah, do you sometimes settle for a superficial repentance that removes only the outer action while the inner attachment remains? Reflect on how the lesson's insight—that true perfection requires the absence of all other defects—calls you to a deeper, more thorough repentance that uproots the very roots of sin.

Two Views on Seeing Allah's Attributes
Al-Nasafi states the two scholarly positions on whether Allah's attributes can be seen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two scholarly positions on whether Allah's attributes can be seen, and the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
Whether attributes are visible depends on what vision can attach to: self-subsistent entities or any existent.

Al-Nasafi presents a theological debate about the visibility of Allah's attributes. While no scriptural evidence explicitly addresses this, rational arguments lead to two different conclusions. He reports the view of the early Maturidi scholars and the view of those who say vision can attach to any existent. Let's examine both positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for the structure. Notice the question and then the two responses introduced by 'فأما' and 'وأما'.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the key terms.
2
Identify the two positions
The first position is attributed to 'قدمائنا' (our early scholars). The second is attributed to 'القائلين بجواز تعلق الرؤية بكل موجود'. Note how each position is linked to a different criterion for visibility.
3
Understand the reasoning
The early scholars argue that vision attaches only to what subsists in itself (قائم بالذات). Attributes do not subsist in themselves, so they cannot be seen. The others say vision attaches to every existent, and attributes are existent, so they can be seen.
This is a purely rational debate, not based on revealed texts.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters in theology. It shows that even within Sunni Islam, there can be differing rational opinions on matters not explicitly settled by revelation.
The author does not take a side here; he simply reports.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[هل صفاته تعالى جائزة الرؤية]
فأما صفاته تعالى فلم يرد دليل سمعي برؤيتها، فأما جواز رؤيتها هل هو ثابت؟ فعلى قول قدمائنا القائلين بجواز تعلق الرؤية بالقائم بالذات واستحالة تعلقها بما ليس بقائم بالذات لم تكن صفات الله تعالى جائزة الرؤية. وأما على قول القائلين بجواز تعلق الرؤية بكل موجود فهي جائزة الرؤية.
[Are His attributes, may He be exalted, permissible to be seen?] As for His attributes, may He be exalted, no transmitted evidence has come for seeing them. As for the permissibility of seeing them, is it established? According to the statement of our early scholars who say that vision attaches only to what subsists in itself and that it is impossible for it to attach to what does not subsist in itself, the attributes of Allah, may He be exalted, are not allowed to be seen. And according to the statement of those who say that vision attaches to every existent, they are allowed to be seen.
Source pages p. 657

Commentary
This passage addresses the question of whether Allah's attributes can be seen. It notes that there is no scriptural evidence for seeing them. The focus is on the rational permissibility. Two positions are presented: (1) The position of the early Maturidi scholars (قدمائنا) who hold that vision can only attach to what is self-subsistent (قائم بالذات), not to attributes which are not self-subsistent, so attributes are not visible. (2) The position of those who say vision attaches to every existent, so attributes are visible. The author does not decide here; he simply reports the two views.

Key Terms

رؤية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that if Allah's essence is visible, His attributes must also be visible.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that the early Maturidi scholars distinguished between self-subsistent and non-self-subsistent, concluding attributes are not visible even if essence is visible.

Checkpoint

B) Because attributes are not self-subsistent and vision cannot attach to them
C) Because attributes are not real
D) Because vision is only for bodies

Key Takeaway
The visibility of Allah's attributes is a rational issue with two opposing views: the early Maturidi view denying it, and the view affirming it based on existence.

?

Refuting the Claim that Divine Command is Foolish
Imam Nasafi refutes the objection that divine command is foolish.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi refutes the objection that divine command (taklīf) is foolish by challenging the analogy between the visible and unseen worlds and providing counterexamples from creation.

Core Idea
Divine command is not foolish; the analogy between visible and unseen worlds fails.

The claim that God's commands are foolish because they bring Him no benefit misunderstands divine transcendence. In this passage, Imam Nasafi systematically dismantles this objection by forcing the opponents to defend their analogy and providing counterexamples from creation. You will see how the argument collapses through a dialectical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection and analogy
Read the opponents' claim: commanding with no benefit and prohibiting with no harm is foolishness in the visible world, so it should be foolishness in the unseen world too.
Note the key assumption: what is true in the visible world must hold in the unseen world.
2
See how Nasafi questions the analogy's validity
Nasafi asks whether everything foolish in the visible world is also foolish in the unseen, and whether what is foolish from one agent is foolish from another. He forces the opponents to either abandon the analogy or provide proof.
3
Examine the counterexample of creation without benefit
Nasafi points out that creating the world without benefit for God is not considered foolish, even though in the visible world such an act would be foolish. This shows the analogy is flawed.
God is not needy, so benefit is irrelevant.
4
Consider the opponents' modified claim and its refutation
The opponents argue that an act in the visible world is only foolish if no one benefits at all. Since creation benefits others, it is not foolish. Nasafi counters with examples of things God created that benefit no one (e.g., hidden parts of the earth), yet these are not foolish.
This refutes the modified claim.
5
Reflect on the final challenge about benefiting others
Nasafi challenges the opponents' claim that benefiting others always removes foolishness in the visible world, using the example of feeding the hungry. He implies that even in the visible world, benefiting others does not automatically make an act non-foolish.
The passage ends mid-argument, but the point stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرد على شبه الخصوم
وأما ما زعموا أن ورود التكليف منه سفه لما فيه من أمر بما لا منفعة فيه للآمر، ونهي عما لا مضرة في وجوده للناهي وهذا سفه.
قيل لهم: لم قلتم: إن هذا سفه؟ فإن قالوا: هذا في الشاهد سفه فكذا يكون في الغائب سفها.
قيل: أكل ما كان في الشاهد من فاعل سفها، كان في الغائب سفها؟ وكذا ما كان من فاعل سفها أكان من غيره سفها؟ فإن قالوا: لا أبطلوا دليلهم، وإن قالوا: نعم، طولبوا بالدليل.
ثم قيل لهم: أليس أن في الشاهد من فعل فعلاً لا منفعة له فيه فهو سفيه، والله تعالى أنشأ العالم وخلقه لا لمنفعة إذ يستحيل عليه الانتفاع لما هو دليل الحاجة وهي نقيصة والنقيصة من أمارات الحدث، ومع ذلك لم يكن سفيها، دل أن التسوية بين الشاهد والغائب غير ثابتة.
فإن قالوا في الشاهد إنما يكون الفاعل لا لمنفعة نفسه سفيها إذ كان غيره لا ينتفع أيضا به، فأما إذا كان ينتفع به غيره فليس بسفيه، والله تعالى فعل ما فعل لينتفع به غيره، وهذا ليس بسفه في الشاهد فلم يكن في الغائب سفها.
قيل: إن الله تعالى خلق أشياء لا ينتفع بها أحد البتة كالأجزاء الداخلة في تخوم الأرض والجبال الشاهقة، فإن أحدًا من الخلائق لا يطلع على تلك الأجزاء ولا يشاهدها لاستحالة دخول شيء من الأشياء في الأجسام المصمتة ليعاين أجزاءها، لم يكن الله تعالى بذلك سفيها. ومن فعل ما لا ينتفع هو به ولا غيره في الشاهد سفيه، فلما افترق الحال بين الشاهد والغائب في هذا الفصل يفترق أيضا في الأمر.
ثم نقول: دعواكم أن في الشاهد من يفعل فعلاً لا ينتفع هو به لم يكن سفها إذ كان ينتفع به غيره قلنا: ليس كذلك بل يعد سفها. فلم قلتم ذلك. فإن قالوا: من أطعم جائعًا أو كسا عاريًا لا يعد سفيها وإن كان لا ينتفع هو به، لما أن غيره ينتفع به وهو
Refutation of the objections of the opponents:
And as for what they claimed, that the occurrence of the divine command (taklīf) from Him is foolishness (safah), because it involves commanding something in which there is no benefit for the commander, and prohibiting something in which there is no harm in its existence for the prohibitor, and this is foolishness.
It was said to them: Why did you say that this is foolishness? If they say: This in the visible world (shāhid) is foolishness, so likewise it is foolishness in the unseen world (ghā'ib).
It was said: Is everything that in the visible world is foolishness from an agent, is it foolishness in the unseen world? And likewise, what is foolishness from one agent, is it foolishness from another? If they say: No, they invalidate their proof, and if they say: Yes, they are asked for the proof.
Then it was said to them: Is it not that in the visible world one who does an act in which there is no benefit for him is foolish, yet God Most High created the world and originated it without benefit, since it is impossible for Him to benefit, because that would indicate need, which is a deficiency, and deficiency is a sign of contingency (ḥudūth), and despite that He was not foolish. This indicates that the equivalence between the visible and the unseen is not established.
If they say: In the visible world, the agent is only considered foolish when he acts without benefit for himself, if no one else also benefits from it. But if someone else benefits from it, he is not foolish. And God Most High did what He did so that others might benefit from it, and this is not foolishness in the visible world, so it is not foolishness in the unseen world.
It was said: God Most High created things from which no one benefits at all, like the parts within the depths of the earth and the lofty mountains. For no creature has access to those parts nor sees them, because it is impossible for anything to enter solid bodies to observe their parts, yet God Most High was not foolish thereby. And one who does something in the visible world from which neither he nor anyone else benefits is foolish. Since the case differs between the visible and the unseen in this particular aspect, it also differs in the matter [at hand].
Then we say: Your claim that in the visible world one who does an act from which he himself does not benefit is not considered foolish if someone else benefits from it — we say: It is not so; rather he is considered foolish. So why did you say that? If they say: One who feeds a hungry person or clothes a naked person is not considered foolish even though he himself does not benefit, because someone else benefits from it, and he
Source pages p. 672

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer refutation of the claim that divine command (taklīf) is foolish (safah). The opponents argue that commanding something with no benefit for God and prohibiting something with no harm for Him is pointless and thus foolish. The author begins by questioning the basis of their claim, forcing them to defend the analogy between the visible world (shāhid) and the unseen (ghā'ib). He then points out that even in the visible world, creating without benefit—such as God's creation of the universe—is not considered foolish because God is free from need. The opponents try to amend their analogy by saying that an act is only foolish if no one benefits at all; since others benefit from creation, it is not foolish. The author counters by giving examples of things God created that benefit no one (e.g., hidden parts of the earth), yet these are not foolish acts. This disproves the opponents' attempted equivalence. Finally, he challenges their claim that benefiting others always exempts one from foolishness in the visible world, citing the counterexample of feeding the hungry, which is not considered foolish—but he leaves the argument open-ended at the text's cutoff.

Key Terms
تكليف (taklīf)
سفه (safah)
شاهد (shāhid)
غائب (ghā'ib)
منفعة (manfa'a)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because God does not benefit from His commands, they are arbitrary or foolish.
Correction: The passage clarifies that divine wisdom does not require personal benefit; God's actions are wise even if they bring Him no benefit, as shown by creation.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection that the author refutes in this passage?
A) That God's commands are impossible because they contradict His essence.
B) That divine command (taklīf) is foolish because it involves no benefit for God.
C) That God cannot command without revealing His will.
D) That the analogy between the visible and unseen worlds is always valid.

Key Takeaway
The analogy between the visible and unseen worlds cannot be used to judge divine actions; God's commands are wise even if they bring Him no personal benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever complained to God that a command you were given seemed pointless or unreasonably difficult? How does this lesson challenge the arrogance behind that complaint, and what is one command you now commit to obey without demanding to see the benefit?
?
When you compare God’s actions to human standards, you are placing yourself as judge over Him. Right now, is there an area of your life (your trials, your unanswered prayers, your duties) where you have secretly judged God as less than wise? Will you repent from that and submit, even if you never see the reason?
?
The inner parts of the earth and the lofty mountains were created for no creature's benefit—yet not in vain. What in your life feels useless or unseen? Will you trust that God's wisdom is at work in those hidden places, and stop demanding that your existence 'make sense' by human logic?

Why God’s Actions Are Not Foolish
Refutes the objection that God's actions are foolish.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that God’s actions could be considered foolish is invalid, because benefit is impossible for God.

Core Idea
Actions need not benefit God to be wise; benefit is impossible for Him.

In this passage, the author presents a hypothetical scenario from human experience to test the definition of foolishness. Someone might think an action is foolish if it brings no personal benefit, even if others benefit. The author then applies this to God, showing that since God cannot benefit at all, the analogy fails. This clarifies that God’s commands and actions are inherently wise.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the objector's claim: an action is not foolish because of benefit to others, but because of personal benefit (praise and reward).
2
Examine the hypothetical example
The objector describes a builder who builds knowing no one will know or reward him, yet others benefit. According to the objector, this builder is foolish.
This shows the criterion is personal benefit, not others' benefit.
3
Identify the refutation
The author states that God does not benefit from His actions due to the impossibility of benefit for Him, so the criterion does not apply to God.
God is not foolish because the definition of foolishness presupposes the possibility of personal benefit.
4
Realize the invalidity of analogy
The attempt to compare God (unseen) to humans (witnessed) fails because the conditions are fundamentally different.
This principle applies to all divine actions, including commands and prohibitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل: إنما لم يعد هذا سفهًا لا لمكان انتفاع غيره بل لمكان انتفاع نفسه فإنه ينال به الثناء الحسن في الدنيا والثواب الموعود في الآخرة، حتى إنه لو بنى مع علمه أن أحدًا من الناس لا يعلم أنه هو الباني فلا يحصل له الثناء، وأخبره نبي من الأنبياء أنه لا ينال الثواب بذلك، ثم بنى مع هذا وأنفق فيه الأموال مع علمه أنه لا حمد له ولا أجر بذلك فإنه يعد سفيها، وإن كان يعلم أن غيره ينتفع به. والله تعالى أنه لا ينتفع بفعله لما مر من استحالة الانتفاع عليه لا يكون سفها، فدل أن اعتبار الغائب في هذا بالشاهد فاسد . فكذا في الأمر والنهي والتكليف.
It was said: This is not considered foolishness not because of the benefit of others, but because of his own benefit, for he attains through it commendation in this world and the promised reward in the hereafter. So much so that if he built knowing that no one among people knows that he is the builder, so he does not obtain commendation, and a prophet among prophets informed him that he does not attain reward by that, then he built despite this and spent wealth upon it knowing that there is no praise for him nor any reward thereby, then he would be considered foolish, even if he knew that others benefit from it. And God Almighty – He does not benefit from His action due to the impossibility of benefit accruing to Him – is not foolish. So this indicates that applying the witnessed to the unseen in this matter is corrupt. So too with commanding, forbidding, and obligation.
Source pages p. 674

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer about the criterion for foolishness (safah). The objector claims that building a ribat in the desert is not considered foolish not because others benefit, but because the builder himself gains praise and reward. If those are absent (no one knows, no reward), the builder is foolish even if others benefit. This would imply that actions without personal benefit are foolish. The author responds that God does not benefit from His actions at all, so the criterion cannot apply to Him. Therefore, the analogy between the witnessed (shahid) world and the unseen (gha'ib) is invalid. The same logic applies to God's commands and prohibitions—they are not foolish even if they bring no benefit to Him.

Key Terms
سفه
شاهد
غائب
انتفاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's actions could be considered foolish if they do not benefit Him or others in some way.
Correction: Foolishness is defined in relation to creatures who can benefit; God does not benefit from anything, so the concept does not apply to Him.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is God's action not considered foolish?
A) Because He benefits from it
B) Because He does not need benefit
C) Because His action is for others' benefit only
D) Because benefit is impossible for Him

Key Takeaway
God's actions are not foolish because benefit is impossible for Him; the human analogy does not apply.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage argues that God does not benefit from His actions. Reflect on a time you obeyed God even though you saw no immediate benefit to yourself. How does this lesson challenge the assumption that your obedience must bring you personal gain?
?
The objector placed human criteria on God. In what area of your life do you find yourself judging God's actions (or lack thereof) by human standards of wisdom or foolishness? What would it mean to truly trust that God's actions are beyond such criteria?
?
The lesson says applying witnessed to unseen is corrupt. How might you be unconsciously applying human logic to God's commands (like expecting ease or reward as a condition)? What is one specific command you struggle with because it seems 'foolish' from a human perspective? How can you realign your thinking?

The Analogy of Command and Divine Wisdom
Nasafi proves divine wisdom by analogy with human commands that benefit others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi argument from command and prohibition establishes that divine wisdom (hikma) is real even though God does not benefit from His commands.

Core Idea
Commands that benefit others are wise, even if the commander gains nothing.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi presents a compelling analogy from everyday life to show that God's commands and prohibitions are truly wise. He asks us to consider a person who warns a blind man about a pit or a lion—that person is not called foolish, even though he gains no personal benefit. Similarly, God's commands are wise because they benefit His servants, not because they benefit Him. This simple yet powerful analogy refutes those who deny divine wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with 'ثم نقول' (Then we say), indicating a new argument.
2
Identify the analogy
Find the example of a person commanding or forbidding something that benefits the commanded person. The passage mentions a blind person being warned about a well or a lion.
This example is crucial for the argument.
3
Understand the opponent's forced admission
The author says the opponent must admit that such a commander is not foolish, otherwise he would be stubborn. Why does this force the opponent's hand?
The example is universally accepted as wise, so denying it would be unreasonable.
4
Apply the analogy to God
The passage then applies this to God: His commands benefit servants and prohibit harm, so they are wisdom, not foolishness, even though God does not benefit.
This directly refutes the opponent's principle that only self-benefiting actions are wise.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that this invalidates the opponent's entire foundation. Consider how this single analogy destroys the opposing view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول: أليس أن من أمر غيره بفعل ينتفع به المأمور أو نهاه عن فعل يندفع الضرر عن المنهي بأن نهى أعمى عن سلوك طريق فيه بئر لئلا يقع فيه، أو دخول بيت فيه أسد ضار، أو حية عظيمة لئلا يفترسه الأسد وتنهشه الحية لم يكن سفيها، فلا بد من بلى لما أنه لو أجاب بغيره لنادى على نفسه بالعناد والمكابرة. فقيل له: ما أنكرت أن أمر الله تعالى بما فيه للعباد منفعة ونهيه عما لهم فيه مضرة حكمة وليس بسفه وإن كان لا ينتفع هو به ولا تنتفي عنه المضرة، وبهذا يبطل أصلكم الذي أصلتموه وتنهدم قاعدتكم التي بنيتم عليها.
Then we say: Is it not that if someone commands another to do an action from which the commanded person benefits, or forbids him an action that repels harm from the forbidden person, such as forbidding a blind person from taking a road that has a well so that he does not fall into it, or entering a house that has a harmful lion, or a large snake so that the lion does not devour him and the snake bites him, he is not considered foolish. So there must be an answer, for if he answered with something else he would call upon himself stubbornness and obstinacy. So it was said to him: What prevents you from acknowledging that the command of God Almighty to what contains benefit for the servants and His prohibition of what contains harm for them is wisdom and not foolishness, even if He does not benefit from it nor is harm repelled from Him? And by this your principle that you have established is invalidated and your foundation that you built upon is destroyed.
Source pages p. 674

Commentary
This passage is a proof by analogy from human custom. The author argues that whenever someone commands another to do something beneficial or forbids something harmful (like warning a blind person of a well or a lion), the commander is not considered foolish, even if he gains no direct benefit. The opponent is forced to admit this because denying it would be stubborn. The author then applies this to God: God's commands and prohibitions are beneficial to His servants, so they are wise (hikma) and not foolish, even though God does not benefit. This refutes the opponent's principle that actions without benefit to the agent are foolish.

Key Terms
أمر
نهي
حكمة
سفه
مأمور
منهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think that divine commands must benefit God to be wise, otherwise they are arbitrary.
Correction: The passage shows that commands are wise if they benefit the commanded, not the commander, as seen in human examples like warning a blind person.

Checkpoint
According to the author, the act of commanding another to do something beneficial is not considered foolish because:
A) The commander benefits
B) The commanded person benefits
C) Both benefit
D) It is not relevant

Key Takeaway
Divine commands are wise because they benefit creatures, not because God benefits.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every command God has given you is for your own good, even when you cannot see the benefit? Examine a specific command you struggle with—are you willing to obey it simply because you trust His wisdom, even if it feels difficult or pointless to you?
?
When you hear a prohibition from God, do you ever resent it or see it as restrictive rather than protective? How would your heart change if you internalized that every 'do not' is a warning like 'do not walk into the well'—a mercy designed to save you from harm?
?
If someone asked you, 'Why does God command this? It doesn't benefit Him,' would you be able to answer with conviction based on this lesson? What would you say, and does your own life reflect that conviction in how you speak about God's laws?

Why Obligation (Taklif) Makes Creation Wise
Refutation of al-Khalī's claim that command without benefit is folly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi response refutes the claim that divine commands without benefit are folly, by showing that obligation (taklif) is what gives creation meaning and wisdom.

Core Idea
Without taklif, creation would be aimless folly; taklif makes it wise.

This lesson focuses on the opening argument against al-Khalī, who argued that commanding without benefit is foolish. The author counters by showing that if there were no obligation (taklif), reward, and punishment, the entire creation would be purposeless and thus folly. Therefore, obligation is precisely what transforms creation from folly into wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage that presents al-Khalī's objection and the author's initial response. Notice how the author first restates the objection before answering.
The objection is that commanding without benefit is folly (safah).
2
Identify the conditional scenario
The author presents a hypothetical: if there were no taklif, no reward/punishment, and the world were not a means to the Hereafter, then creation would be purposeless and thus folly. This establishes the need for taklif.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that since creation without taklif would be folly, and since God is wise, taklif must exist to make creation wise. Anyone who thinks otherwise is ignorant.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد خرج الجواب عما تعلق به الخليع، أن الأمر والنهي بما لا نفع للأمر في تحصيله ولا ضرر على الناهي في فعل ما نهى عنه سفه، إذ قد بينا في أثناء كلامنا أن التكليف لو لم يكن وانعدم الثواب والعقاب ولم تصر الدنيا مطية إلى الآخرة التي فيها يجازى المحسن بإحسانه والمسيء بإساءته، صار تخليقه العالم ليوجد ثم يتلاشى ويثبت ثم ينعدم، ويفنى من غير أن يتعلق به غرض أو يحصل له عاقبة حميدة. وهذا النوع من الفعل سفه يذم فاعله، وتأبى الحكمة إطلاقه، وذا غير جائز على الله تعالى. فإذا صار تخليق العالم بواسطة التكليف حكمة، فمن ظن ذلك سفها فهو جاهل، فكذا في أفعال الخلق ما هو قبيح، فلو لم ينه عنه الصانع لكان ذلك إباحة له إذ الإباحة هي زوال الحظر والأمر عن محتمل التكليف، وإباحة القبيح سفه. ومما يؤيد هذا أن في القول بإزالة التكليف والثواب والعقاب إعطاء الأمان للنفس الأمارة بالسوء عن عواقب المطالبات، وإغراء للنفس السبعية التي من دأبها القهر والغلبة على العبث والفساد والنهب والإغارة، وسفك الدماء وتخريب البلاد وحصول التفاني وهذا كله سفه وورود المنع عن ذلك كله حكمة، فمن ظن أن المنع عن سفه والإطلاق له حكمة فقد قلب القصة. يحققه أنه تعالى لو لم يأمر عباده بشكر ما أنعم عليهم ولم ينه عن كفرانه لكان فيه تضييع النعم وذا سفه، فلابد من الأمر بالشكر والنهي عن الكفران على ما سبق ذكره، وذلك هو التكليف. وقد سبق فى أول الفصل ما يوجب بطلان القول ببطلان التكليف فلا معنى للإعادة. ثم نقول: هل الأمر بما لا نفع للآمر بفعله والنهي عما لا ضرر على الناهي بفعله سفه في الشاهد أم لا؟ فإن قال: لا، أبطل كلامه لأن جعل ذلك في الغائب سفة، لدلالة كون مثله سفها في الشاهد. وإن قال نعم هو سفه. قيل: أحسن هو أم قبيح؟ فإن قال: هو حسن عرف العقلاء مبلغ عقله حيث زعم أن السفه حسن وإن قال: هو قبيح. قيل: أمباح أم محظور أم واجب؟ فإن قال بالوجوب أو الإباحة. فقيل: من الذي أوجبه عليه أو أباحه له؟ فإن قال: هو الله تعالى، فقد أعظم القول، حيث زعم أن الله تعالى يبيح القبيح أو يوجبه، على أنه مع بطلانه ترك لمذهبه لأن الإيجاب أو الإباحة
And the answer to what al-Khalī' clung to has come out, that commanding and forbidding that which has no benefit for the commander in its attainment and no harm to the forbidder in the act of what he forbade is folly, for we have already made clear in the midst of our speech that if obligation (taklif) were not, and reward and punishment were absent, and the world was not made a mount to the Hereafter in which the good-doer is rewarded for his goodness and the evildoer for his evil, then His creating the world to exist then vanish, and to be established then ceasing, and to perish without any purpose attached to it or any praiseworthy consequence resulting from it—this type of act is folly for which its doer is blamed, and wisdom refuses to allow it, and that is not permissible for God Exalted. So when the creation of the world becomes wisdom by means of obligation, then whoever thinks that it is folly is ignorant. Likewise, among the acts of creatures there is what is evil. So if the Maker did not forbid it, that would be permission of it, because permission is the removal of prohibition and command concerning one capable of obligation, and permission of evil is folly. And what supports this is that the claim of removing obligation, reward and punishment gives security to the soul commanding to evil from the consequences of demands, and incites the bestial soul whose habit is oppression and dominance to frivolity, corruption, plunder, raiding, bloodshed, destroying lands, and annihilation; all of this is folly, and the occurrence of prohibition against all that is wisdom. So whoever thinks that prohibition is folly and free rein is wisdom has reversed the matter. And it verifies that if He Exalted did not command His servants to thank Him for what He bestowed upon them and did not forbid ingratitude towards it, there would be waste of blessings in that, and that is folly. So command of gratitude and prohibition of ingratitude are necessary, as previously mentioned, and that is obligation. And what necessitates the falsity of the claim of invalidity of obligation has preceded at the beginning of the chapter, so there is no meaning to repetition. Then we say: Is commanding that which has no benefit for the commander in its performance and forbidding that which involves no harm to the forbidder in its performance considered folly in the witnessed world or not? If he says: No, he invalidates his own speech because making that folly in the unseen is [invalid] due to the evidence that such a thing is folly in the witnessed. And if he says: Yes, it is folly. It is said: Is it good or evil? If he says: It is good, the intelligent know the extent of his intellect, as he claims that folly is good. And if he says: It is evil. It is said: Is it permissible, prohibited, or obligatory? If he says obligatory or permissible. It is said: Who made it obligatory upon him or permissible for him? If he says: It is God Exalted. Then he has uttered a grave statement, where he claims that God Exalted permits or obligates evil, while besides its falsehood, he has abandoned his own school, because obligation or permission
Source pages p. 691

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the objection raised by al-Khalī' who claimed that commanding and forbidding without benefit or harm is folly. The author responds by first establishing that without obligation (taklif) and reward/punishment, creation would be purposeless and thus folly. So obligation makes creation wise. Then he applies this to human evil actions: if God did not forbid evil, that would be permission of evil, which is folly. He further supports this by noting that removing obligation gives free rein to the evil-commanding soul, leading to corruption, while forbidding it is wisdom. Next, he provides a verification: if God did not command gratitude and forbid ingratitude, that would waste blessings, so commands and prohibitions are necessary. Finally, he enters a dialectical argument: he asks whether commanding without benefit is folly in the witnessed world. If the opponent says no, he contradicts himself. If he says yes, then the author asks a series of questions to trap him: is it good or evil? If good, he is foolish. If evil, then is it permissible, prohibited, or obligatory? If he says obligatory or permissible, then who made it so? If he says God, then he attributes evil to God, which is false and against his own school (Mu'tazila who hold God does not command evil). The passage is cut off at the end but the argument is clear.

Key Terms
تکلیف
سفه
حکمة
الخلیع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's commands without apparent benefit are arbitrary or foolish.
Correction: The author shows that commands (taklif) give purpose to creation; without them, creation would be foolish, so commands are necessary for wisdom.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what would be the result if there were no obligation (taklif)?
A) The world would be eternal.
B) Creation would be purposeless and thus folly.
C) People would still be rewarded.
D) God would still be wise.

Key Takeaway
Taklif (obligation) is what transforms an otherwise purposeless creation into a wise and meaningful one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that some of Allah's commands are pointless or burdensome? How does this lesson challenge that feeling, and what will you do to change your perspective starting today?
?
If you truly believed that your obedience serves no purpose beyond this life, would you still strive to obey? How does knowing that your taklif gives meaning to all of creation affect your commitment to it?
?
Think of a time you neglected an obligation because it seemed to have no immediate benefit. In light of this lesson, how will you remind yourself that every command is part of a wise plan, and what step will you take to act on that reminder this week?

The Wisdom of Prohibition and the Dialectical Trap
Refutation of al-Khalī extending to human acts and dialectical argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author argues that forbidding evil is wisdom, not folly, and how he uses a dialectical series of questions to corner the opponent.

Core Idea
Permitting evil is folly; forbidding it is wisdom; and those who think otherwise are caught in a contradiction.

This lesson covers the second part of the refutation, where the author applies the same logic to human evil acts. If God did not forbid evil, that would be permission of evil, which is folly. He then supports this with the argument that removing obligation gives free rein to the lower soul, causing corruption. Finally, he enters a dialectical exchange, asking whether commanding without benefit is folly in the witnessed world, and uses the opponent's answer to trap him into attributing evil to God or contradicting himself.

Guided Path
1
Read the application to human acts
Read the passage that extends the argument from divine acts to human acts. The author states that if the Maker did not forbid evil among creatures, that would be permission of evil, and permission of evil is folly.
This parallels the earlier argument about creation's purpose.
2
Identify the supporting argument
The author adds that removing obligation gives security to the evil-commanding soul and incites bestial behavior, leading to widespread corruption. Forbidding these is wisdom.
This is a practical argument about human nature.
3
Follow the dialectical questions
The author then poses a series of questions: Is commanding without benefit folly in the witnessed world? If the opponent says no, he contradicts himself. If yes, is it good or evil? If good, he is foolish. If evil, is it permissible, prohibited, or obligatory? If obligatory or permissible, who made it so? If he says God, he attributes evil to God, which is false.
This traps the opponent into an absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد خرج الجواب عما تعلق به الخليع، أن الأمر والنهي بما لا نفع للأمر في تحصيله ولا ضرر على الناهي في فعل ما نهى عنه سفه، إذ قد بينا في أثناء كلامنا أن التكليف لو لم يكن وانعدم الثواب والعقاب ولم تصر الدنيا مطية إلى الآخرة التي فيها يجازى المحسن بإحسانه والمسيء بإساءته، صار تخليقه العالم ليوجد ثم يتلاشى ويثبت ثم ينعدم، ويفنى من غير أن يتعلق به غرض أو يحصل له عاقبة حميدة. وهذا النوع من الفعل سفه يذم فاعله، وتأبى الحكمة إطلاقه، وذا غير جائز على الله تعالى. فإذا صار تخليق العالم بواسطة التكليف حكمة، فمن ظن ذلك سفها فهو جاهل، فكذا في أفعال الخلق ما هو قبيح، فلو لم ينه عنه الصانع لكان ذلك إباحة له إذ الإباحة هي زوال الحظر والأمر عن محتمل التكليف، وإباحة القبيح سفه. ومما يؤيد هذا أن في القول بإزالة التكليف والثواب والعقاب إعطاء الأمان للنفس الأمارة بالسوء عن عواقب المطالبات، وإغراء للنفس السبعية التي من دأبها القهر والغلبة على العبث والفساد والنهب والإغارة، وسفك الدماء وتخريب البلاد وحصول التفاني وهذا كله سفه وورود المنع عن ذلك كله حكمة، فمن ظن أن المنع عن سفه والإطلاق له حكمة فقد قلب القصة. يحققه أنه تعالى لو لم يأمر عباده بشكر ما أنعم عليهم ولم ينه عن كفرانه لكان فيه تضييع النعم وذا سفه، فلابد من الأمر بالشكر والنهي عن الكفران على ما سبق ذكره، وذلك هو التكليف. وقد سبق فى أول الفصل ما يوجب بطلان القول ببطلان التكليف فلا معنى للإعادة. ثم نقول: هل الأمر بما لا نفع للآمر بفعله والنهي عما لا ضرر على الناهي بفعله سفه في الشاهد أم لا؟ فإن قال: لا، أبطل كلامه لأن جعل ذلك في الغائب سفة، لدلالة كون مثله سفها في الشاهد. وإن قال نعم هو سفه. قيل: أحسن هو أم قبيح؟ فإن قال: هو حسن عرف العقلاء مبلغ عقله حيث زعم أن السفه حسن وإن قال: هو قبيح. قيل: أمباح أم محظور أم واجب؟ فإن قال بالوجوب أو الإباحة. فقيل: من الذي أوجبه عليه أو أباحه له؟ فإن قال: هو الله تعالى، فقد أعظم القول، حيث زعم أن الله تعالى يبيح القبيح أو يوجبه، على أنه مع بطلانه ترك لمذهبه لأن الإيجاب أو الإباحة
And the answer to what al-Khalī' clung to has come out, that commanding and forbidding that which has no benefit for the commander in its attainment and no harm to the forbidder in the act of what he forbade is folly, for we have already made clear in the midst of our speech that if obligation (taklif) were not, and reward and punishment were absent, and the world was not made a mount to the Hereafter in which the good-doer is rewarded for his goodness and the evildoer for his evil, then His creating the world to exist then vanish, and to be established then ceasing, and to perish without any purpose attached to it or any praiseworthy consequence resulting from it—this type of act is folly for which its doer is blamed, and wisdom refuses to allow it, and that is not permissible for God Exalted. So when the creation of the world becomes wisdom by means of obligation, then whoever thinks that it is folly is ignorant. Likewise, among the acts of creatures there is what is evil. So if the Maker did not forbid it, that would be permission of it, because permission is the removal of prohibition and command concerning one capable of obligation, and permission of evil is folly. And what supports this is that the claim of removing obligation, reward and punishment gives security to the soul commanding to evil from the consequences of demands, and incites the bestial soul whose habit is oppression and dominance to frivolity, corruption, plunder, raiding, bloodshed, destroying lands, and annihilation; all of this is folly, and the occurrence of prohibition against all that is wisdom. So whoever thinks that prohibition is folly and free rein is wisdom has reversed the matter. And it verifies that if He Exalted did not command His servants to thank Him for what He bestowed upon them and did not forbid ingratitude towards it, there would be waste of blessings in that, and that is folly. So command of gratitude and prohibition of ingratitude are necessary, as previously mentioned, and that is obligation. And what necessitates the falsity of the claim of invalidity of obligation has preceded at the beginning of the chapter, so there is no meaning to repetition. Then we say: Is commanding that which has no benefit for the commander in its performance and forbidding that which involves no harm to the forbidder in its performance considered folly in the witnessed world or not? If he says: No, he invalidates his own speech because making that folly in the unseen is [invalid] due to the evidence that such a thing is folly in the witnessed. And if he says: Yes, it is folly. It is said: Is it good or evil? If he says: It is good, the intelligent know the extent of his intellect, as he claims that folly is good. And if he says: It is evil. It is said: Is it permissible, prohibited, or obligatory? If he says obligatory or permissible. It is said: Who made it obligatory upon him or permissible for him? If he says: It is God Exalted. Then he has uttered a grave statement, where he claims that God Exalted permits or obligates evil, while besides its falsehood, he has abandoned his own school, because obligation or permission
Source pages p. 691

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the objection raised by al-Khalī' who claimed that commanding and forbidding without benefit or harm is folly. The author responds by first establishing that without obligation (taklif) and reward/punishment, creation would be purposeless and thus folly. So obligation makes creation wise. Then he applies this to human evil actions: if God did not forbid evil, that would be permission of evil, which is folly. He further supports this by noting that removing obligation gives free rein to the evil-commanding soul, leading to corruption, while forbidding it is wisdom. Next, he provides a verification: if God did not command gratitude and forbid ingratitude, that would waste blessings, so commands and prohibitions are necessary. Finally, he enters a dialectical argument: he asks whether commanding without benefit is folly in the witnessed world. If the opponent says no, he contradicts himself. If he says yes, then the author asks a series of questions to trap him: is it good or evil? If good, he is foolish. If evil, then is it permissible, prohibited, or obligatory? If he says obligatory or permissible, then who made it so? If he says God, then he attributes evil to God, which is false and against his own school (Mu'tazila who hold God does not command evil). The passage is cut off at the end but the argument is clear.

قبيح
نفس أمارة بالسوء
سفه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that forbidding evil is restrictive and thus foolish.
Correction: The author shows forbidding evil prevents chaos and is wisdom; permitting evil would be folly.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would be the consequence if God did not forbid evil actions?
A) They would be considered good.
B) They would be permitted, and permission of evil is folly.
C) They would be impossible.
D) They would become obligatory.

Key Takeaway
Forbidding evil is wisdom because it prevents harm and corruption; permitting evil would be folly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you currently tolerating any evil in your life—whether in your own actions, relationships, or environment—because you have not clearly forbidden it? What would it cost you to speak or act against it, and are you willing to pay that price for wisdom?
?
The passage warns that removing obligation gives free rein to the soul commanding evil. Where have you loosened the obligations of your faith, convincing yourself it is freedom, when in fact you have given your lower soul permission to cause harm?
?
If God's silence on an evil action would be permission, what does your silence in the face of wrongdoing around you reveal about your own heart? Today, commit to forbidding one specific evil you have been ignoring.
